Defining Dalalah and Distinguishing it from Fahm
Author defines dalalah and distinguishes it from fahm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dalalah as a property of the indicator and fahm as a property of the understander, and identify the implication that something is called an indicator before understanding occurs.

Core Idea
Dalalah belongs to the indicator; fahm belongs to the understander—they are not the same.

This lesson clarifies the fundamental definitions that underpin the study of indication in Islamic logic. The author begins by distinguishing between dalalah (indication) as a property of the indicator and fahm (understanding) as a property of the understander. This distinction has a practical consequence: something can be called an 'indicator' even before understanding takes place, based solely on its property of indicating. Let's walk through this definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definienda
Locate in the Arabic text where the author defines الدلالة (indication) and الفهم (understanding). Note that الدلالة is attributed to الدال (the indicator) and الفهم to الفاهم (the understander).
This is a key terminological distinction.
2
Extract the genus
Both الدلالة and الفهم are described as صفة (properties). The genus is 'property' or 'attribute'. The differentia for each is what it is a property of: الدلالة is a property of the indicator, الفهم is a property of the understander.
This is a minimal definition, but sufficient for logical analysis.
3
Analyze the implication
The author says that based on the two meanings, something is called 'indicator' in reality according to the first meaning (الدلالة as property of indicator) even before understanding occurs. Understand why: if الدلالة is a property of the indicator, it exists whether or not someone actually understands. This is a logical consequence of the definition.
This shows that indication is objective, not dependent on a particular act of understanding.
4
Distinguish the two definitions
Contrast the first meaning (الدلالة as property of the indicator) with the second meaning (الفهم as property of the understander). The second meaning would not make something an indicator before understanding because understanding is the actual comprehension. This distinction prevents confusion between the indicator and the act of understanding.
The author emphasizes that the intended fruit (الثمرة المقصودة) is understanding, but the definition of indication remains on the side of the indicator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عَنِ السَّيْدِ؛ قَالَ: إِذْ لَا اشْتِبَاهَ فِي أَنَّ الدَّلَالَةَ صِفَةُ الأَمْرِ الدَّالّ ، وَالفَهْمُ صِفَةُ الفَاهِمِ، وَكَأَنَّهُمْ نَبَّهُوا بِهَذَا التَّسَامُحِ عَلَى أَنَّ الثَّمَرَةَ المَقْصُودَةَ هِيَ الفَهْمُ . وَيَنْبَنِي عَلَى المَعْنَيْنِ المَذْكُورَيْنِ : أَنَّ الأَمْرَ قَبْلَ حُصُولِ الفَهْمِ مِنْهُ بِالفِعْلِ يُقَالُ لَهُ : «دَالٌ» حَقِيقَةٌ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ دُونَ الثَّانِي وَلَا بُدَّ فِي الدَّلَالَةِ عِنْدَ أَهْلِ هَذَا الفَنْ مِنِ اطردادِهَا، وَلِهَذَا قَالَ السَّيِّدُ : الدَّلَالَةُ المُعْتَبَرَةُ فِي هَذَا الفَنِّ مَا كَانَتْ كُلِّيَّةٌ ، وَأَمَّا إِذَا فُهِمَ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ مَعْنَى فِي بَعْضِ الأَوْقَاتِ بِوَاسِطَةِ قَرِينَةٍ، فَأَهْلُ هَذَا الفَنِّ لا يَحْكُمُونَ بِأَنَّهُ دَالٌ عَلَيْهِ بِخِلَافِ أَصْحَابِ العَرَبِيَّةِ وَالأُصُولِ . لَكِنَّ الَّذِي صَرَّحَ بِهِ السَّعْدُ فِي شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ أَنَّ المَجَازَ يَدُلُّ عَلَى مَعْنَاهُ المَجَازِيِّ . الأنبابي: قوله : (أَنَّ المَجَازَ يَدُلُّ عَلَى مَعْنَاهُ المَجَازِي) فحينئذٍ دلالة اللفظ على تمام المعنى المجازي مطابقة، ودلالته على جزء ذلك المعنى المجازي تضمُّن، وعلى لازمه التزام فتكون أقسام الدلالة في المعنى المجازي كالحقيقي، وهو الذي حققه السَّعد في المطوّل، وصرَّح به السَّنوسي في «شرح إيساغوجي» وجرى عليه في شرح المختصر.
From al-Sayyid, he said: Since there is no confusion that indication is a property of the indicator, and understanding is a property of the understander. And it is as if they pointed out by this tolerance that the intended fruit is understanding. And it follows from the two mentioned meanings that something before obtaining understanding from it actually is called 'indicator' in reality according to the first meaning but not the second. And indication in this art must be universal. For this reason al-Sayyid said: The indication considered in this art is that which is universal. But if a meaning is understood from a word at some times by means of a contextual clue, then the people of this art do not judge that it is an indicator of it, contrary to the experts in Arabic and Usul. However, that which al-Sa'd explicitly stated in his Sharh al-Shamsiyya is that the metaphorical indicates its metaphorical meaning. [al-Anbabi: His statement that 'the metaphorical indicates its metaphorical meaning' then the indication of the word for the whole metaphorical meaning is by correspondence, its indication for a part of that metaphorical meaning is by inclusion, and its indication for a necessary concomitant is by implication. Thus the divisions of indication for the metaphorical meaning are like those for the real meaning. This is what al-Sa'd verified in al-Mutawwal, and al-Sanusi explicitly stated in his Sharh Isaghuji, and it is followed in his Sharh al-Mukhtasar.]
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage begins by distinguishing between dalalah (indication) as a property of the indicator (dal) and fahm (understanding) as a property of the understander (fahim). It then draws an important implication: something is called 'indicator' in reality based on the first meaning (that dalalah is a property of the indicator) even before understanding occurs. The second meaning (understanding) would not make it an indicator before understanding. The passage then establishes a key condition for dalalah in the art of logic: it must be universal (kulliyya), meaning it must hold in all cases, not just occasionally. Al-Sayyid al-Sharif is cited as stating this condition. The text notes that if a word only indicates a meaning sometimes due to a contextual clue, scholars of logic do not consider it an indicator of that meaning, unlike scholars of Arabic grammar and Usul al-Fiqh. However, a potential contradiction arises: al-Sa'd al-Taftazani explicitly states in his Sharh al-Shamsiyya that the metaphorical (majaz) indicates its metaphorical meaning. The commentator al-Anbabi resolves this by explaining that the metaphorical meaning does indeed have the same three types of indication (mutabaqa, tadamun, iltizam) as the real meaning, and thus it is still universal in the sense that it is a habitual indication, not a one-off occurrence. This resolution is supported by al-Sa'd's own verification in al-Mutawwal and by al-Sanusi.

The Condition of Universality and the Indication of Majaz
Author sets condition of universality and addresses majaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition of universality for dalalah in logic and explain how metaphorical indication satisfies this condition through habitual use.

Core Idea
For logicians, dalalah must be universal; occasional indication from context does not count, but metaphorical indication is universal when habitual.

This lesson examines a key condition that the logicians impose on dalalah: it must be universal (kulliyya). The author contrasts this with occasional understanding, which does not qualify as dalalah in the art of logic. He then addresses an apparent problem: how can metaphorical meaning (majaz) indicate if it depends on context? The commentator al-Anbabi resolves this by showing that metaphorical indication also has the three types (mutabaqa, tadamun, iltizam) and is habitual, not occasional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Find in the Arabic where the author states: 'ولا بد في الدلالة عند أهل هذا الفن من اطرادها' and 'الدلالة المعتبرة في هذا الفن ما كانت كلية'. This establishes that dalalah must be universal (kulliyya) in the art of logic.
Universality means that the indication holds in all ordinary uses, not just sometimes.
2
Contrast with occasional indication
The author notes that if a meaning is understood from a word only some of the time due to a contextual clue (قرينة), the logicians do not consider that word an indicator of that meaning. Understand the criterion: universal vs. occasional.
This is different from the approach of grammarians and usul scholars, who may accept such occasional indications.
3
Understand the problem with majaz
Al-Sa'd al-Taftazani stated that the metaphorical (المجاز) does indicate its metaphorical meaning. But metaphorical meaning often relies on context. How can it be universal? This creates a tension with the condition.
The passage quotes al-Anbabi to resolve this.
4
Analyze al-Anbabi's resolution
Al-Anbabi explains that the metaphorical meaning has the same three types of indication: مطابقة (correspondence for the whole meaning), تضمن (inclusion for a part), التزام (implication for a necessary concomitant). The key is that this indication is habitual (مطرد) in the usage of the language, not a one-off. Thus it meets the universality condition.
This resolution is supported by al-Sa'd in al-Mutawwal and al-Sanusi in his commentaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عَنِ السَّيْدِ؛ قَالَ: إِذْ لَا اشْتِبَاهَ فِي أَنَّ الدَّلَالَةَ صِفَةُ الأَمْرِ الدَّالّ ، وَالفَهْمُ صِفَةُ الفَاهِمِ، وَكَأَنَّهُمْ نَبَّهُوا بِهَذَا التَّسَامُحِ عَلَى أَنَّ الثَّمَرَةَ المَقْصُودَةَ هِيَ الفَهْمُ . وَيَنْبَنِي عَلَى المَعْنَيْنِ المَذْكُورَيْنِ : أَنَّ الأَمْرَ قَبْلَ حُصُولِ الفَهْمِ مِنْهُ بِالفِعْلِ يُقَالُ لَهُ : «دَالٌ» حَقِيقَةٌ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ دُونَ الثَّانِي وَلَا بُدَّ فِي الدَّلَالَةِ عِنْدَ أَهْلِ هَذَا الفَنْ مِنِ اطردادِهَا، وَلِهَذَا قَالَ السَّيِّدُ : الدَّلَالَةُ المُعْتَبَرَةُ فِي هَذَا الفَنِّ مَا كَانَتْ كُلِّيَّةٌ ، وَأَمَّا إِذَا فُهِمَ مِنَ اللَّفْظِ مَعْنَى فِي بَعْضِ الأَوْقَاتِ بِوَاسِطَةِ قَرِينَةٍ، فَأَهْلُ هَذَا الفَنِّ لا يَحْكُمُونَ بِأَنَّهُ دَالٌ عَلَيْهِ بِخِلَافِ أَصْحَابِ العَرَبِيَّةِ وَالأُصُولِ . لَكِنَّ الَّذِي صَرَّحَ بِهِ السَّعْدُ فِي شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ أَنَّ المَجَازَ يَدُلُّ عَلَى مَعْنَاهُ المَجَازِيِّ . الأنبابي: قوله : (أَنَّ المَجَازَ يَدُلُّ عَلَى مَعْنَاهُ المَجَازِي) فحينئذٍ دلالة اللفظ على تمام المعنى المجازي مطابقة، ودلالته على جزء ذلك المعنى المجازي تضمُّن، وعلى لازمه التزام فتكون أقسام الدلالة في المعنى المجازي كالحقيقي، وهو الذي حققه السَّعد في المطوّل، وصرَّح به السَّنوسي في «شرح إيساغوجي» وجرى عليه في شرح المختصر.
From al-Sayyid, he said: Since there is no confusion that indication is a property of the indicator, and understanding is a property of the understander. And it is as if they pointed out by this tolerance that the intended fruit is understanding. And it follows from the two mentioned meanings that something before obtaining understanding from it actually is called 'indicator' in reality according to the first meaning but not the second. And indication in this art must be universal. For this reason al-Sayyid said: The indication considered in this art is that which is universal. But if a meaning is understood from a word at some times by means of a contextual clue, then the people of this art do not judge that it is an indicator of it, contrary to the experts in Arabic and Usul. However, that which al-Sa'd explicitly stated in his Sharh al-Shamsiyya is that the metaphorical indicates its metaphorical meaning. [al-Anbabi: His statement that 'the metaphorical indicates its metaphorical meaning' then the indication of the word for the whole metaphorical meaning is by correspondence, its indication for a part of that metaphorical meaning is by inclusion, and its indication for a necessary concomitant is by implication. Thus the divisions of indication for the metaphorical meaning are like those for the real meaning. This is what al-Sa'd verified in al-Mutawwal, and al-Sanusi explicitly stated in his Sharh Isaghuji, and it is followed in his Sharh al-Mukhtasar.]

The Six Types of Indication (Dalala)
Enumeration of six types of indication with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any example of indication into one of the six types by identifying its relation (conventional, rational, habitual) and medium (verbal/non-verbal).

Core Idea
Indication is analyzed by two dimensions: relation cause and sign type.

The author lays out the foundational taxonomy of indication (dalala) that underlies all discussions of meaning in Islamic logic and legal theory. Understanding this six-fold division allows you to precisely identify the type of evidential force different signs carry, whether in Quranic exegesis, prophetic traditions, or everyday language.

Guided Path
1
Understand the primary division
The author first divides indication into three types based on the relation: conventional (wad'iyya), rational ('aqliyya), and habitual ('adiyya). Note that these are mutually exclusive categories.
2
Apply the secondary division
Within each of the three, the indicator (dal) can be either a word (lafz) or something other than a word (ghayr al-lafz). This gives 2x3=6 types.
The passage lists them in order: (1) verbal conventional, (2) verbal rational, (3) verbal habitual, (4) non-verbal conventional, (5) non-verbal rational, (6) non-verbal habitual.
3
Map the examples to the grid
For each example given, identify which row (conventional/rational/habitual) and which column (verbal/non-verbal) it belongs to. For instance, 'lion indicating the predatory animal' is verbal conventional.
The author's examples: (1) لفظ وضعية, (2) لفظ عقلية, (3) لفظ عادية, (4) غير لفظ وضعية, (5) غير لفظ عقلية, (6) غير لفظ عادية.
4
Test with your own examples
Think of a sign from Islamic law (e.g., the word 'prayer' indicating the ritual) or theology (e.g., the universe's order indicating a Creator) and classify it.
Practice makes the taxonomy intuitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلِلدَّلَالَةِ سِتَّةُ أَقْسَامِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا إِمَّا وَضْعِيَّةٌ، أَوْ عَقْلِيَّةٌ، أَوْ عَادِيَّةٌ، وَعَلَى كُلِّ الدَّالُ : إِمَّا لَفْظُ، أَوْ غَيْرُهُ.
(١) فَدَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ الوَضْعِيَّةِ كَدَلَالَةِ الأَسَدِ عَلَى الحَيَوَانِ المُفْتَرِسِ .
(٢) وَالعَقْلِيَّةِ : دَلَالَةِ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى وُجُودِ لَافِظِهِ، أَوْ حَيَاتِهِ .
(٣) وَالعَادِيَّةِ كَدَلَالَةِ أَخْ عَلَى الوَجَعِ مُطلَقاً، وَأَحّ عَلَى وَجَعِ الصَّدْرِ .
(٤) وَدَلَالَةُ غَيْرِ اللَّفْظِ الوَضْعِيَّةِ كَدَلَالَةِ الإِشَارَةِ بِالرَّأْسِ إِلَى أَسْفَلِ عَلَى مَعْنَى نَعَمْ، وَإِلَى أَعْلَى عَلَى مَعْنَى لا .
(٥) وَالعَقْلِيَّةِ كَدَلَالَةِ تَغَيُّرِ العَالَمِ عَلَى حُدُوثِهِ .
(٦) وَالعَادِيَّةِ كَدَلَالَةِ الحُمْرَةِ عَلَى الخَجَلِ، وَالصُّفْرَةِ عَلَى الوَجَلِ .
And for indication there are six divisions, because it is either conventional, or rational, or habitual, and for each the indicator is either a word or other than a word. (1) The conventional indication of a word is like the indication of 'lion' upon the predatory animal. (2) And rational: the indication of a word upon the existence of its utterer, or his life. (3) And habitual: like the indication of 'akh' upon pain absolutely, and 'ahh' upon chest pain. (4) And the conventional indication of other than a word is like the indication of pointing with the head downwards upon the meaning of 'yes', and upwards upon the meaning of 'no'. (5) And rational: like the indication of change of the world upon its temporal origination. (6) And habitual: like the indication of redness upon shyness, and yellowness upon fear.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage defines the six types of indication (dalala). The author first divides indication into three categories based on the nature of the relation: conventional (wad'iyya), where the relation is by human convention; rational ('aqliyya), where the relation is by pure reason (e.g., effect implying cause); and habitual ('adiyya), where the relation is based on custom or constant conjunction. Each category is then subdivided into verbal (lafz) and non-verbal (ghayr al-lafz). The author provides concrete examples: for verbal conventional, 'lion' indicating the predatory animal; for verbal rational, the word indicating the existence or life of its speaker (since speech requires a living speaker); for verbal habitual, the exclamation 'akh' indicating pain in general and 'ahh' indicating chest pain. For non-verbal conventional, head gestures for 'yes' and 'no'; for non-verbal rational, the change of the world indicating its createdness (a standard kalam argument); for non-verbal habitual, blushing indicating shyness and paleness indicating fear. These examples clarify the distinctions and anchor them in familiar contexts from everyday language and theological reasoning.

الوصعية
العقلية
العادية
اللفظ

Key Takeaway
Indication (dalala) is systematically divided into six types based on the cause of the relation (convention, reason, habit) and the nature of the sign (verbal or non-verbal).

Three Types of Verbal Conventional Indication
Author defines three types of verbal indication and asserts rational enumeration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three types of verbal conventional indication (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and justify why the enumeration is considered rational.

Core Idea
The word indicates its meaning in three exhaustive ways: the whole, a part, or a necessary concomitant.

Logicians restrict their study to conventional verbal indication. This passage clarifies that the author's classification into three types is rationally exhaustive, based on the nature of meaning itself. Understanding these types is fundamental for analyzing definitions and arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of logicians' study
Notice that the passage begins by stating logicians only research the first division of the six types of indication—verbal conventional indication. The author uses the rhetorical device ihtibak to imply the qualifier 'verbal' even where it is omitted.
The six types are: verbal conventional, verbal intellectual, verbal natural, non-verbal conventional, non-verbal intellectual, non-verbal natural. Only the first concerns logicians.
2
Extract the three types from the text
The passage explicitly lists three types: mutabaqa (correspondence), tadammun (implication by inclusion), and iltizam (implication by necessary concomitance). Write them down with their definitions as given.
Mutabaqa: word indicates the whole meaning it was placed for (e.g., 'lion' for the animal). Tadammun: indicates a part of that meaning (e.g., 'house' for the roof). Iltizam: indicates something external but necessarily connected (e.g., 'house' for the builder).
3
Understand the rational basis for the enumeration
Al-Sayyid (al-Jurjani) argues the enumeration is rational ('aqli) because a word must indicate either its primary meaning (mutabaqa), a part of that meaning (tadammun), or something external to it (iltizam). There is no fourth possibility.
This is a rational classification because it derives from the nature of signification, not from empirical observation.
4
Apply the classification to a concrete example
Take the Arabic word 'بيت' (house). Identify what it indicates under each type: mutabaqa: the entire house; tadammun: the roof; iltizam: the builder. This shows how the same word can have multiple indications.
In Islamic legal theory, these types help determine what a term in a scripture or legal text actually signifies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمَنَاطِقَةُ إِنَّمَا يَبْحَثُونَ عَنِ الأَوَّلِ مِنْ هَذِهِ الأَقْسَامِ، وَهُوَ مُرَادُ المُصَنِّفِ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُصَرِّح بِالتَّقْيِيدِ بـ «اللَّفْظِيَّةِ»؛ لِأَخْذِهِ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ إلخ»، فَيَكُونُ قَدْ حَذَفَ هُنَا قَيْدَ اللَّفْظِيَّةِ» وَأَثْبَتَهُ فِيمَا يَأْتِي، كَمَا أَنَّهُ حَذَفَ ثَمَّ قَيْدَ الوَضْعِيَّةِ وَأَثْبَتَهُ هُنَا ، فَفِي كَلَامِهِ : احْتِبَاكُ .
وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ أَنْوَاعَ هَذَا القِسْمِ ثَلَاثَةٌ ، كَمَا يُعْلَمُ مِنِ اسْتِقْصَاءِ كَلَامِهِ . وَالحَصْرُ فِيهَا عَقْلِيٌّ كَمَا قَالَهُ السَّيِّدُ ؛ لأنَّ اللَّفْظَ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَدُلُّ عَلَى المَعْنَى المَوْضُوعِ لَهُ، أَوْ عَلَى جُزْئِهِ، أَوْ عَلَى خَارِجِهِ . وَاسْتَشْكَلَ القَرَافِيُّ هَذَا الحَصْرَ بِدَلَالَةِ العَامِّ عَلَى بَعْضِ أَفْرَادِهِ كَـ : «عَبِيدِي»؛ لِأَنَّ بَعْضَ أَفْرَادِهِ لَمْ يُوضَعْ لَهُ اللَّفْظُ حَتَّى تَكُونَ: «مُطَابَقَةٌ، وَلَيْسَ جُزْءاً حَتَّى تَكُونَ: «تَضَمُّناً»، وَلَا خَارِجاً حَتَّى تَكُونَ: «الْتِزَامَاً».
And the logicians only research the first of these divisions, and it is the intention of the author even if he did not explicitly restrict it to 'verbal', because it is taken from his saying: 'indication of the word' etc. So he has omitted here the restriction 'verbal' and mentioned it in what comes, just as he omitted there the restriction 'conventional' and mentioned it here. So in his speech there is intertwining (ihtibak). And it is not hidden that the types of this division are three, as is known from tracing his words. And the enumeration of them is rational as al-Sayyid said, because the word: either indicates the meaning it was placed for, or upon its part, or upon something external to it. And al-Qarafi objected to this enumeration with the indication of the general (al-amm) upon some of its individuals like 'my slaves', because some of its individuals were not placed for it (the word) so that it would be mutabaqa, nor is it a part so that it would be tadammun, nor is it external so that it would be iltizam.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage first clarifies that logicians (al-mantiqiyyun) are only concerned with the first division of the six: verbal conventional indication (dalalat al-lafz al-wad'iyya). The author (probably al-Bajuri) notes that even though the author of the matn (likely al-Sullam or similar) did not explicitly attach the qualifier 'verbal' here, it is implied from his expression 'indication of the word'. There is a rhetorical device called ihtibak where a term is omitted in one place and mentioned in another, and vice versa. Next, it states that the types of this division (verbal conventional indication) are three: mutabaqa (correspondence), tadammun (implication by inclusion), and iltizam (implication by necessary concomitance). This classification is rational ('aqli), as said by al-Sayyid (probably al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani), because the word either indicates its primary meaning (mutabaqa), a part of that meaning (tadammun), or something necessarily connected to it outside the meaning (iltizam). For example, 'house' indicates the whole structure (mutabaqa), the roof (tadammun), and the builder (iltizam). Then the passage presents an objection by al-Qarafi, who challenges the exhaustiveness of this threefold division using the case of a general term like 'my slaves' (عبدي). The general term 'my slaves' indicates some individuals (a subset of the referents), but those individuals are not the entire meaning placed for it (so not mutabaqa), they are not a part of the meaning (because the meaning is a universal, not a whole composed of parts), and they are not an external concomitant (if it were external, it would apply to all individuals equally, leaving no specific indication). This objection highlights a subtle issue in the classification: the relation of the general to its partial instances. The passage ends without providing the answer, leaving it as a challenge.

Key Terms
مطابقة
تضمن
التزام
حصر عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the enumeration of the three types being rational?
A) Because the word can only indicate the meaning it was placed for, a part of it, or something external to it.
B) Because al-Sayyid said so.
C) Because the logicians agreed upon it.
D) Because it is derived from the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
The three types of verbal conventional indication are exhaustive and rationally derived from the nature of words.

Al-Qarafi's Objection: The Problem of General Terms
Author presents al-Qarafi's objection to the threefold classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Qarafi's objection to the threefold classification and evaluate whether the classification remains exhaustive for general terms.

Core Idea
Al-Qarafi challenges the rational enumeration by asking: what about a general term indicating some of its individuals?

The threefold classification of verbal indication seems clean, but al-Qarafi raises a sophisticated objection: general terms like 'my slaves' indicate some individuals, yet these individuals are not the full meaning, not a part, and not an external concomitant. This lesson unpacks the objection and its implications for logic and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Al-Qarafi gives the example of a general term like 'عَبِيدِي' (my slaves). The word indicates some of its individuals (a subset of all slaves). The question is: which type of indication is this?
General terms (عام) refer to all individuals of a category, but the word can also indicate a partial group in context.
2
Analyze why it does not fit the three types
The passage argues: It is not mutabaqa because the word was placed for the entire universal, not for a subset. It is not tadammun because the individuals are not parts of the meaning (the meaning is a universal, not a whole with parts). It is not iltizam because the connection between the general term and some individuals is not a necessary concomitant (it would apply to all individuals if it were).
This reveals a potential gap: word might indicate something that is neither the whole meaning, nor a part, nor an external necessary concomitant.
3
Consider implications for the classification
If the objection holds, the threefold classification is not exhaustive. The passage does not provide an answer, leaving it as a challenge for the reader. Reflect on possible responses: Could this be a case of tadammun if the universal is seen as a whole and individuals as parts? Or could it be a distinct type?
This shows the dynamic nature of logical discourse; objections refine and test classifications.
4
Relate to usul al-fiqh
In Islamic legal theory, general terms (عام) are fundamental. How a legal text like 'أَحِلَّتْ لَكُمْ بَهِيمَةُ الأَنْعَامِ' (cattle are permitted to you) indicates each individual animal affects rulings. Al-Qarafi's objection reminds us that the logical foundation of 'amm indication needs careful analysis.
Usul scholars often treat the indication of 'amm for its individuals as mutabaqa, because the meaning of 'amm includes all individuals. But al-Qarafi's objection challenges this assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمَنَاطِقَةُ إِنَّمَا يَبْحَثُونَ عَنِ الأَوَّلِ مِنْ هَذِهِ الأَقْسَامِ، وَهُوَ مُرَادُ المُصَنِّفِ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُصَرِّح بِالتَّقْيِيدِ بـ «اللَّفْظِيَّةِ»؛ لِأَخْذِهِ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ إلخ»، فَيَكُونُ قَدْ حَذَفَ هُنَا قَيْدَ اللَّفْظِيَّةِ» وَأَثْبَتَهُ فِيمَا يَأْتِي، كَمَا أَنَّهُ حَذَفَ ثَمَّ قَيْدَ الوَضْعِيَّةِ وَأَثْبَتَهُ هُنَا ، فَفِي كَلَامِهِ : احْتِبَاكُ .
وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ أَنْوَاعَ هَذَا القِسْمِ ثَلَاثَةٌ ، كَمَا يُعْلَمُ مِنِ اسْتِقْصَاءِ كَلَامِهِ . وَالحَصْرُ فِيهَا عَقْلِيٌّ كَمَا قَالَهُ السَّيِّدُ ؛ لأنَّ اللَّفْظَ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَدُلُّ عَلَى المَعْنَى المَوْضُوعِ لَهُ، أَوْ عَلَى جُزْئِهِ، أَوْ عَلَى خَارِجِهِ . وَاسْتَشْكَلَ القَرَافِيُّ هَذَا الحَصْرَ بِدَلَالَةِ العَامِّ عَلَى بَعْضِ أَفْرَادِهِ كَـ : «عَبِيدِي»؛ لِأَنَّ بَعْضَ أَفْرَادِهِ لَمْ يُوضَعْ لَهُ اللَّفْظُ حَتَّى تَكُونَ: «مُطَابَقَةٌ، وَلَيْسَ جُزْءاً حَتَّى تَكُونَ: «تَضَمُّناً»، وَلَا خَارِجاً حَتَّى تَكُونَ: «الْتِزَامَاً».
And the logicians only research the first of these divisions, and it is the intention of the author even if he did not explicitly restrict it to 'verbal', because it is taken from his saying: 'indication of the word' etc. So he has omitted here the restriction 'verbal' and mentioned it in what comes, just as he omitted there the restriction 'conventional' and mentioned it here. So in his speech there is intertwining (ihtibak). And it is not hidden that the types of this division are three, as is known from tracing his words. And the enumeration of them is rational as al-Sayyid said, because the word: either indicates the meaning it was placed for, or upon its part, or upon something external to it. And al-Qarafi objected to this enumeration with the indication of the general (al-amm) upon some of its individuals like 'my slaves', because some of its individuals were not placed for it (the word) so that it would be mutabaqa, nor is it a part so that it would be tadammun, nor is it external so that it would be iltizam.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage first clarifies that logicians (al-mantiqiyyun) are only concerned with the first division of the six: verbal conventional indication (dalalat al-lafz al-wad'iyya). The author (probably al-Bajuri) notes that even though the author of the matn (likely al-Sullam or similar) did not explicitly attach the qualifier 'verbal' here, it is implied from his expression 'indication of the word'. There is a rhetorical device called ihtibak where a term is omitted in one place and mentioned in another, and vice versa. Next, it states that the types of this division (verbal conventional indication) are three: mutabaqa (correspondence), tadammun (implication by inclusion), and iltizam (implication by necessary concomitance). This classification is rational ('aqli), as said by al-Sayyid (probably al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani), because the word either indicates its primary meaning (mutabaqa), a part of that meaning (tadammun), or something necessarily connected to it outside the meaning (iltizam). For example, 'house' indicates the whole structure (mutabaqa), the roof (tadammun), and the builder (iltizam). Then the passage presents an objection by al-Qarafi, who challenges the exhaustiveness of this threefold division using the case of a general term like 'my slaves' (عبدي). The general term 'my slaves' indicates some individuals (a subset of the referents), but those individuals are not the entire meaning placed for it (so not mutabaqa), they are not a part of the meaning (because the meaning is a universal, not a whole composed of parts), and they are not an external concomitant (if it were external, it would apply to all individuals equally, leaving no specific indication). This objection highlights a subtle issue in the classification: the relation of the general to its partial instances. The passage ends without providing the answer, leaving it as a challenge.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qarafi's objection challenges the exhaustiveness of the threefold classification, showing the need for a more nuanced understanding of indication.

Does al-Qarafi's objection apply here? How might a defender of the threefold classification respond? Write a short defense.

The Three Types of Signification and Their Conventionality
Text defines dalala al-mutabaqa and classifies the three types with dispute over their conventionality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish dalala al-mutabaqa, al-tadammun, and al-iltizam, and identify whether each type is considered conventional or intellectual according to the two main scholarly views.

Core Idea
Words signify meanings in three ways: correspondence, inclusion, and implication — and scholars debate whether the latter two are conventional or intellectual.

In this passage, the author defines dalala al-mutabaqa and then classifies the three types of signification. He then presents the dispute over whether tadammun and iltizam are conventional (wad'iyya) or intellectual ('aqliyya). Understanding this classification is crucial for grasping how words convey meanings in Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
Find in the Arabic the terms for the three types: mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam. Note that mutabaqa is defined first as 'signification of a word for what agrees with it'.
2
Define each type based on the passage
Mutabaqa: a word signifies its entire meaning. Tadammun: a word signifies a part of its meaning (e.g., 'house' signifies 'roof'). Iltizam: a word signifies something necessarily implied by its meaning (e.g., 'ceiling' implies 'wall').
The passage does not give full definitions but implies these through the classification.
3
Understand the two views on conventionality
First view (al-Amidi, Ibn al-Hajib, etc.): tadammun is wad'iyya, iltizam is 'aqliyya. Second view: mutabaqa is wad'iyya by agreement, iltizam is 'aqliyya without dispute, but tadammun is disputed (some say wad'iyya, some say 'aqliyya).
The passage also notes that the dispute is verbal (lafzi) because both sides concede a role for convention and intellect.
4
Recognize the verbal nature of the dispute
Al-Ghanimi and others state that those who call tadammun and iltizam 'aqliyya do not deny convention has a role, and those who call them wad'iyya do not deny they require an intellectual premise. Thus the disagreement is over terminology.
Logicians call them wad'iyya, while rhetoricians call them 'aqliyya, due to different definitions of 'intellectual'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع الدلالة الوضعية
(٢٤) دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَا وَافَقَهُ يَدْعُونَهَا: «دَلَالَةَ المُطَابَقَهُ»
الباجوري
أَنَّ التَّضَمُّنِيَّةَ وَضْعِيَّةٌ وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةَ عَقْلِيَّةٌ ، وَهَذِهِ إِحْدَى الطَّرِيقَتَيْنِ فِي ذَلِكَ . وَالأُخْرَى أَنَّ المُطَابِقِيَّةَ وَضْعِيَّةٌ اتفاقاً كَالأَوْلَى، والالتزامِيَّةَ عَقْلِيَّةٌ بِلَا خِلَافٍ، وَأَمَّا التَّضَمُّنِيَّةُ فَقِيلَ : وَضْعِيَّةٌ، وَقِيلَ : عَقْلِيَّةٌ . اهـ مُلَخَّصاً مِنْ شَرْحِ الشَّيْخِ المَلْوِيِّ مَعَ زِيَادَة .
قَوْلُهُ : (دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ) أَي : الوَضْعِيَّةِ ، كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (عَلَى مَا وَافَقَهُ) أَيْ: عَلَى مَعْنِى أَوِ الَّذِي وَافَقَ ذَلِكَ اللَّفْظُ، فَـ «مَا : نَكِرَةٌ مَوْصُوفَةٌ، أَوْ مَعْرِفَةٌ مَوْصُولَةٌ .
الأنبابي
قوله : (أَنَّ التَّضَمُّنِيَّةَ وَضْعِيَّةٌ وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةَ عَقْلِيَّةٌ) هذا هو الذي جرى عليه الآمدي، وابن الحاجب، وابن الهمام، وغيرهم مِنَ المحققين .
ووجه ـ كما في «الكبير (۳) ـ: بأنَّ التَّضمن فهم الجزء في ضمن الكلِّ؛ إذ لا شك أنَّه إذا فُهِمَ المعنى فُهِمَت أَجزاؤه . فليس فيها انتقال مِنَ اللَّفظ إلى المعنى، ومِنَ المعنى إلى الجزء، بل هو فهم واحد يسمى بالقياس إلى تمام المعنى : «مطابقة»، وبالقياس إلى جزئه : «تضمناً، بخلاف دلالة الالتزام، فإنَّه لا بدَّ فيها مِنَ الانتقال مِنَ اللَّفظ إلى المعنى، ومِنَ المعنى إلى اللازم ضرورة أنَّ اللازم لا دخل له في الوضع أَصلاً.
ووجه أيضاً : بأنَّ الجزء داخل فيما وضع له اللفظ، بخلاف اللازم، فإنَّه خارج عنه، وصرح غير واحدٍ كالغنيمي بأنَّ الخلاف لفظي، فإنَّ مَن قال «بعقليتهما لا ينكر أنَّ للوضع دخلاً فيهما، ومن قال بوضعيتهما لا ينكر توقفهما على مقدّمة عقلية، فالخلاف في التسمية.
وفي حاشية السيرامي على المطوّل : أنَّ المنطقيين سمُّوا التَّضمُّنيَّة والالتزامية: «وضعية»، وإن كان للعقل مدخل فيهما ؛ لتخصيصهم العقليَّة بالصرفة، وسمَّاهما البيانيُّون : «عقليَّةً»، وإن كان للوضع مدخل فيهما ؛ لعدم تخصيصهم العقلية بالصرفة . اهـ
Types of conventional signification. (24) The signification of a word for what agrees with it they call 'mutabaqa signification.' Al-Bajuri: That tadammuniyya is conventional and iltizamiyya is intellectual, and this is one of the two ways regarding that. The other is that mutabaqiyya is conventional by agreement like the first, and iltizamiyya is intellectual without dispute, and as for tadammuniyya, it is said: conventional, and said: intellectual. End, summarized from al-Shaykh al-Malwi's commentary with addition. His statement: (signification of the word) i.e., conventional, as known from what preceded. And his statement: (for what agrees with it) i.e., to a meaning or that which that word agreed, so 'ma' is an indefinite described or a definite relative. Al-Anbabi: His statement: (that tadammuniyya is conventional and iltizamiyya is intellectual) is what al-Amidi, Ibn al-Hajib, Ibn al-Humam, and other investigators followed. And the reason — as in al-Kabir — is that tadammun is understanding the part within the whole; for no doubt that when the meaning is understood, its parts are understood. So there is no transfer from the word to the meaning and from the meaning to the part, but it is a single understanding called in relation to the complete meaning 'mutabaqa' and in relation to its part 'tadammun', unlike iltizam signification, for there must be transfer from the word to the meaning and from the meaning to the necessary concomitant, necessarily because the concomitant has no part in the convention at all. And also a reason: that the part is included in what the word is set for, unlike the necessary concomitant, for it is outside it. And more than one, like al-Ghanimi, stated that the dispute is verbal, for whoever says they are intellectual does not deny that convention has a role in them, and whoever says they are conventional does not deny that they depend on an intellectual premise, so the dispute is in terminology. And in al-Sirami's gloss on al-Mutawwal: that the logicians called tadammuniyya and iltizamiyya 'conventional', even if the intellect has a role in them, because they restrict 'intellectual' to the pure; and the rhetoricians called them 'intellectual', even if convention has a role in them, because they do not restrict 'intellectual' to the pure. End.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage defines dalala al-mutabaqa (signification by correspondence) and then classifies the three types of signification: mutabaqa, tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (implication). It also presents the dispute over whether tadammun and iltizam are conventional (wad'iyya) or intellectual ('aqliyya). The text begins by stating the definition: a word's signification of what agrees with it is called mutabaqa. Then al-Bajuri presents two ways regarding the classification. The first way: tadammun is conventional and iltizam is intellectual. The second way: mutabaqa is conventional by agreement, iltizam is intellectual without dispute, and tadammun is disputed (some say conventional, some say intellectual). This is summarized from al-Malwi's commentary. Then al-Bajuri parses the wording: 'dalala al-lafz' means conventional signification, and 'ala ma wafaqahu' means on a meaning that agrees with the word. Then al-Anbabi explains the reasoning behind the first view. He states that this view was followed by al-Amidi, Ibn al-Hajib, Ibn al-Humam, and other investigators. The reason is that tadammun (inclusion) involves understanding the part within the whole; when the whole meaning is understood, its parts are simultaneously understood without additional mental transfer. This is a single act of understanding that is called mutabaqa relative to the entire meaning and tadammun relative to the part. In contrast, iltizam requires a mental transfer from the word to the meaning and then from the meaning to the necessary concomitant (lazim), because the concomitant is not included in the original convention. Another reason is that the part is included in what the word was set for, while the concomitant is outside it. Al-Anbabi then notes that al-Ghanimi and others stated that the dispute is verbal (lafzi), because those who call tadammun and iltizam intellectual do not deny that convention has a role, and those who call them conventional do not deny that they depend on an intellectual premise. So the dispute is merely about terminology. Finally, a quote from al-Sirami's gloss explains that logicians call them conventional because they restrict 'intellectual' to pure intellectual signification, while rhetoricians call them intellectual because they do not restrict 'intellectual' to the pure.

Key Takeaway

Three Types of Conventional Signification and the Condition of Perspective
Author defines the three types and explains the perspective condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam, and apply the condition of haythiyya to classify any given signification correctly.

Core Idea
The same word signifies differently depending on which placement perspective we adopt.

In Islamic logic, every conventionally placed word (wad') can signify its meaning in three ways: as a whole, as a part, or as a necessary concomitant. But without specifying the perspective (haythiyya), these three types would collapse into one another. In this passage, the author introduces the haythiyya condition and shows how it preserves the distinctness of each type using the example of the word 'sun'. Mastering this distinction is crucial for precise analysis of definitions and arguments in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the three definitions
Identify the three types: mutabaqa (word signifies its entire meaning as placed), tadammun (word signifies a part of that meaning), iltizam (word signifies a necessary concomitant of that meaning). Write them down with clear examples.
2
Understand the haythiyya condition
The author adds the phrase 'min haythu' (insofar as) to each definition. This means we must consider the word's placement (wad') when classifying. Without this, a word might appear to fit multiple definitions simultaneously.
For example, 'sun' placed for the whole (body+light) can signify the body alone as tadammun, not mutabaqa, because the body is a part of the whole meaning.
3
Apply to the sun example systematically
Work through the four cases: (1) word 'sun' placed for whole → body alone = tadammun, not mutabaqa. (2) word placed for body alone → body alone = mutabaqa, but then light is iltizam. (3) word placed for whole → light alone = tadammun. (4) word placed for body alone → light = iltizam. Confirm that each case respects the perspective.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Take the word 'salah' (prayer). It was placed for a specific set of actions. Consider its signification: the whole set (mutabaqa), the act of ruku' (tadammun), and the obligation of avoiding sin (iltizam). Determine the correct classification from the perspective of the original placement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَا وَافَقَهُ يَدْعُونَهَا: «دَلَالَةَ المُطَابَقَةِ» وَجُزْئِهِ: «تَضَمُّنًا»، وَمَا لَزِمَ فَهُوَ الْتِزَامُ. فَبِقَيْدِ الحَيثِيَّةِ المَذْكُورَةِ يَخْرُجُ مَا ذُكِرَ عَنْ تَعْرِيفِ دَلَالَةِ المُطَابَقَةِ؛ لِأَنَّ دَلَالَةَ لَفْظِ «الشَّمْسِ» عَلَى مَا ذُكِرَ لَيْسَتْ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ مَعْنَاهُ، بَلْ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ جُزْءُ مَعْنَاهُ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ، وَمِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ لَازِمُ مَعْنَاهُ عَلَى الثَّانِي. وَلِأَنَّهُ إِذَا نُظِرَ لِوَضْعِهِ لِلجِرْمِ وَحْدَهُ، تَكُونُ دَلَالَتُهُ عَلَيْهِ: دَلَالَةَ مُطَابَقَةٍ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهَا تَعْرِيفُ دَلَالَةِ التَّضَمُّنِ؛ لِأَنَّ اللَّفْظَ قَدْ دَلَّ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ بِالنَّظَرِ لِوَضْعِهِ لِلْمَجْمُوعِ. وَتَكُونُ دَلَالَتُهُ عَلَى الضَّوْءِ وَحْدَهُ: دَلَالَةَ الْتِزَامٍ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهَا تَعْرِيفُ دَلَالَةِ التَّضَمُّنِ؛ لِأَنَّ اللَّفْظَ قَدْ دَلَّ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ بِالنَّظَرِ لِذَلِكَ. فَبِقَيْدِ الحَيثِيَّةِ المَذْكُورَةِ يَخْرُجُ مَا ذُكِرَ عَنْ تَعْرِيفِ دَلَالَةِ التَّضَمُّنِ؛ لِأَنَّ دَلَالَةَ لَفْظِ «الشَّمْسِ» عَلَى مَا ذُكِرَ لَيْسَتْ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ جُزْءُ مَعْنَاهُ، بَلْ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ مَعْنَاهُ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ، وَمِنْ حَيْثُ لَازِمُ مَعْنَاهُ عَلَى الثَّانِي. وَإِذَا نُظِرَ لِوَضْعِهِ لِلضَّوْءِ وَحْدَهُ، تَكُونُ دَلَالَتُهُ عَلَيْهِ: دَلَالَةَ مُطَابَقَةٍ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهَا تَعْرِيفُ دَلَالَةِ الْاِلْتِزَامِ؛ لِأَنَّ اللَّفْظَ قَدْ دَلَّ عَلَى لَازِمٍ مَعْنَاهُ بِالنَّظَرِ لِوَضْعِهِ لِلجِرْمِ وَحْدَهُ. وَإِذَا نُظِرَ لِوَضْعِهِ لِلْمَجْمُوعِ، تَكُونُ دَلَالَتُهُ عَلَى الضَّوْءِ وَحْدَهُ: دَلَالَةَ تَضَمُّنٍ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهَا تَعْرِيفُ دَلَالَةِ الْاِلْتِزَامِ؛ لِأَنَّ اللَّفْظَ قَدْ دَلَّ عَلَى لَازِمٍ مَعْنَاهُ بِالنَّظَرِ لِذَلِكَ. فَبِقَيْدِ الحَيثِيَّةِ المَذْكُورَةِ يَخْرُجُ مَا ذُكِرَ عَنْ تَعْرِيفِ دَلَالَةِ الْاِلْتِزَامِ؛ لِأَنَّ دَلَالَةَ لَفْظِ «الشَّمْسِ» عَلَى مَا ذُكِرَ لَيْسَتْ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ لَازِمُ مَعْنَاهُ، بَلْ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ مَعْنَاهُ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ، وَمِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ جُزْءُ مَعْنَاهُ عَلَى الثَّانِي.
The signification of a word for what corresponds to it—they call it 'signification of agreement.' And for a part of it—'signification of inclusion.' And what is necessary—it is 'signification of implication.' So by the mentioned condition of perspective, what was mentioned is excluded from the definition of signification of agreement; because the signification of the word 'sun' for what was mentioned is not insofar as it is its meaning, but rather insofar as it is a part of its meaning in the first case, and insofar as it is a necessary concomitant of its meaning in the second case. And because if one considers its placement for the body alone, its signification for it is signification of agreement, even though the definition of signification of inclusion applies to it; because the word has signified a part of its meaning by consideration of its placement for the whole. And its signification for the light alone is signification of implication, even though the definition of signification of inclusion applies to it; because the word has signified a part of its meaning by consideration of that. So by the mentioned condition of perspective, what was mentioned is excluded from the definition of signification of inclusion; because the signification of the word 'sun' for what was mentioned is not insofar as it is a part of its meaning, but rather insofar as it is its meaning in the first case, and insofar as it is a necessary concomitant of its meaning in the second case. And if one considers its placement for the light alone, its signification for it is signification of agreement, even though the definition of signification of implication applies to it; because the word has signified a necessary concomitant of its meaning by consideration of its placement for the body alone. And if one considers its placement for the whole, its signification for the light alone is signification of inclusion, even though the definition of signification of implication applies to it; because the word has signified a necessary concomitant of its meaning by consideration of that. So by the mentioned condition of perspective, what was mentioned is excluded from the definition of signification of implication; because the signification of the word 'sun' for what was mentioned is not insofar as it is a necessary concomitant of its meaning, but rather insofar as it is its meaning in the first case, and insofar as it is a part of its meaning in the second case.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of conventional signification (dalala wad'iyya) and clarifies a critical condition—the 'haythiyya' or perspective—that must be considered when classifying signification. The author begins by stating the standard definitions: signification of agreement (mutabaqa) is when a word signifies its entire meaning as placed; signification of inclusion (tadammun) is when it signifies a part of that meaning; signification of implication (iltizam) is when it signifies something necessarily entailed by the meaning. However, the author then introduces a subtle point: these definitions must be taken with the condition that the signification is from the perspective of the word's placement (wad'). Without this condition, the example of the word 'sun' (shams) would cause confusion. The word 'sun' was originally placed for the entire celestial body (jirm) and its light (daw'). If a person hears 'sun' and understands only the body, that understanding comes from the perspective of the word's placement for the whole (body+light), not from a separate placement for the body alone. Thus, understanding the body alone is actually a signification of inclusion (tadammun) relative to the whole meaning, not mutabaqa. Similarly, understanding the light alone is also tadammun if considered from the whole placement, but could be iltizam if considered from the body placement (since light necessarily accompanies the body). The author systematically shows that each potential classification (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) for the word 'sun' with respect to body or light individually is valid only if we consider the appropriate perspective. The condition 'min haythu' (insofar as) excludes these cases from the definitions when the signification is not from the intended perspective. In essence, the passage teaches that the type of signification is not an intrinsic property of the word-meaning relation but depends on which placement we are considering. This is a foundational distinction in Islamic logic and semantics.

Key Terms
دلالة المطابقة
دلالة التضمن
دلالة الالتزام
الحيثية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the word 'sun' is considered from the perspective of its placement for the whole (body+light), its signification for the body alone is which type?
A) Mutabaqa, because the body is the main meaning.
B) Tadammun, because the body is a part of the whole meaning.
C) Iltizam, because the body necessarily accompanies the light.
D) None, because the word was not placed for the body alone.

Key Takeaway
The haythiyya condition ensures that each type of signification is determined by the perspective of the word's original placement, preventing definitional overlap.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the word 'sawm' (fasting) as placed in the Shari'ah for the entire act of abstaining from dawn to sunset with intention. Identify whether understanding 'abstaining from food' alone is mutabaqa, tadammun, or iltizam, and specify the perspective used.
?
Take the word 'zakat' (obligatory charity). If someone hears 'zakat' and understands only the monetary payment, without the intention, what type of signification is it from the perspective of the original wad'? Explain how the haythiyya condition clarifies this.

Understanding Tadammun Signification: The Whole and Its Parts
Author defines tadammun signification and refutes an objection using an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of tadammun signification using al-Mallawi's example and evaluate the objections raised against it.

Core Idea
Tadammun: the whole signifies its part, but the mind grasps the whole first.

In this lesson, we explore tadammun (inclusion) signification, where a word like 'human' signifies its genus 'animal' by inclusion. The commentator defines it and then addresses an objection: if understanding a composite requires understanding its parts, how can we transition from the composite to a part? Al-Mallawi provides a concrete example from everyday reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of tadammun
Find the matn line that defines tadammun (التضمن) as signification of the part by inclusion. The commentator explains that it is so named because the meaning includes its part, based on the rule that the whole includes the part.
The Arabic term is التضمن, and it is contrasted with الالتزام (implication).
2
Understand the objection
Some object: understanding a composite requires understanding its parts first, so how can one transition from the composite to its part? This seems to violate the principle that parts precede the whole in knowledge.
3
Analyze al-Mallawi's example
Al-Mallawi illustrates with a scenario: you see a distant shape, doubt if it is an animal, then are told it is a human. You immediately understand it is an animal (a part of 'human') without focusing on rationality. This shows the mind first grasps the whole meaning 'human' in a general way, then focuses on the part 'animal'.
The example shows that the transition is from a general understanding of the whole to a specific part, not from ignorance.
4
Examine the two objections to the example
First objection: this would imply the whole is mentally prior to the part, but they agree the part is prior in both mental and external existence. Second: it would require understanding the part twice (once within the whole, once separately), which direct experience contradicts.
These objections challenge the coherence of tadammun, but the commentator defends it by noting that the initial grasp is vague and the part is not fully understood until the transition.
5
Reflect on the resolution
The example shows that there is no logical contradiction: the whole can be understood in a general sense before its parts are distinguished. The mind moves from the general to the specific, which is a common cognitive process.
This defends the legitimacy of tadammun as a valid type of signification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَجُزْئِهِ: «تَضَمُّناً»، وَمَا لَزِمْ فَهُوَ: «الْتِزَامُ» إِنْ بِعَقْلِ الْتَزَمْ
وَسُمِّيتْ بِذَلِكَ؛ لِتَضَمُّنِ المَعْنَى لِجُزْئِهِ، لأَنَّ القَاعِدَةَ: «أَنَّ الكُلَّ يَتَضَمَّنُ الجُزْء». وَقَدِ اسْتَشْكَلَ بَعْضُهُمْ ذَلِكَ : بِأَنَّ فَهْمَ المُرَكَبِ بِفَهُم أَجْزَائِهِ ، فَكَيْفَ يَتَأَتَى الانْتِقَالُ مِنَ المُرَكَبِ إِلَى جُزْئِهِ؟!
وَصَوَّرَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ بِمَا إِذَا رَأَيْتَ شَبَحاً مِنْ بُعْدِ، وَشَكَكْتَ فِيهِ هَلْ هُوَ حَيَوَانُ أَوْ لَا؟ فَقِيلَ لَكَ : هُوَ إِنْسَانٌ، فَفَهِمْتَ أَنَّهُ حَيَوَانُ، وَلَمْ تَلْتَفِتْ إِلَى كَوْنِهِ نَاطِقاً، وَإِنْ كَانَ يَقَعُ فِي الذِّهْنِ أَوَّلاً المَعْنَى بِتَمَامِهِ؛ قَالَ : فَهَذَا مِثَالٌ يَظْهَرُ فِيهِ الانْتِقَالُ مِنْ مَعْنَى اللَّفْظِ إِلَى جُزْئِهِ ، إِذْ لَا مَانِعَ مِنْ أَنْ يُفْهَمَ المَعْنَى إِجْمَالاً ، ثُمَّ يَنتَقِلُ الذِّهْنُ إِلَى جُزْنِهِ.
وَيُحِثَ فِيهِ مِنْ وَجْهَيْنِ : الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ يَسْتَلْزِمُ تَقَدُّمَ الكُلِّ عَلَى الجُزْءِ ذِهْناً ، مَعَ اتَّفَاقِهِمْ عَلَى تَقَدُّمِ الجُزْءِ عَلَى الكُلِّ في الوُجُودَيْنِ ؛ أَعْني : الوُجُودَ الذهني وَالوُجُودَ الخَارِجِيَّ . وَالثَّانِي : أَنَّهُ يَسْتَلْزِمُ أَنْ يُفْهَمَ الجُزْءُ مَرَّتَيْنِ ؛ مَرَّةٌ فِي ضِمْنِ الكُلِّ وَأُخْرَى مُنْفَرِداً ، وَالوُجْدَانُ يُكَذِّبُهُ.
And its part, by inclusion. And what is necessary, then it is implication if it is bound by the mind. It is named thus because the meaning includes its part, for the rule is that the whole includes the part. Some have objected to that: because the understanding of the composite is through understanding its parts, so how can the transition from the composite to its part occur? Al-Mallawi illustrated it with: when you see a figure from afar and doubt whether it is an animal or not, and it is said to you: it is a human, so you understand that it is an animal, without paying attention to its being rational, even if the complete meaning first occurs in the mind. He said: This is an example in which the transition from the meaning of the expression to its part appears, for there is no obstacle to understanding the meaning in a general way, then the mind transitions to its part. And he objects to it from two aspects: first, it entails the priority of the whole over the part mentally, while they agree on the priority of the part over the whole in both existences: I mean mental existence and external existence. Second, it entails that the part is understood twice: once within the whole and another separately, and direct experience denies it.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage defines tadammun (inclusion) signification and addresses an objection. The matn line states: 'And its part, by inclusion. And what is necessary, then it is implication if it is bound by the mind.' The commentator then explains that tadammun is so called because the meaning includes its part, as the whole includes the part. Some object: understanding a composite is through understanding its parts, so how can we say we transition from composite to part? Al-Mallawi provides an example: seeing a distant shape, doubting if it is an animal, then being told it is a human; you immediately understand it is an animal (the genus) without focusing on rationality (the differentia). Thus, the complete meaning 'human' (animal + rational) first occurs in the mind generally, then the mind singles out the part 'animal'. Two objections follow: (1) This would imply the whole mentally precedes the part, but they agree the part precedes the whole in both mental and external existence. (2) It would require understanding the part twice—once within the whole and once separately—but direct experience contradicts this. The passage thus defends the validity of tadammun despite the objections.

الالتزام
فهم المركب

C) Understanding the definition of 'human' as 'rational animal'.
D) Understanding 'animal' from 'human' after considering all parts.

Key Takeaway
Tadammun signification allows a term to signify its part by inclusion, and the mind's initial grasping of the whole does not prevent later focusing on the part, as shown in al-Mallawi's example.

Implicative and Necessary Signification: Definition and Defense
Definitions of implicative and necessary signification with objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between implicative and necessary signification and explain the logical objections raised against the definition of implicative signification along with their resolutions.

Core Idea
Implicative signification is one understanding without transition; necessary requires intellectual transition.

This passage from Bajuri's commentary on Bannani defines two crucial types of signification in Islamic logic: implicative (tadammun) and necessary (iltizam). The author clarifies that implicative signification does not involve a transition from whole to part, unlike necessary signification which necessarily does. He then addresses two objections that could confuse these distinctions, providing sophisticated answers that safeguard the integrity of the definitions.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two definitions
Locate where the author defines implicative signification as 'understanding of the part within the whole' and necessary signification as 'signification of a necessary concomitant requiring intellectual transition'. Note that implicative is about part-whole; necessary is about an external concomitant.
Implicative: no transition; necessary: transition required.
2
Examine the one-understanding thesis
Observe the claim that implicative signification is a single understanding, not two. Understand that when one hears 'human', they understand the whole (animal+rational) all at once, and the part is understood within that whole without a separate step.
This distinguishes it from necessary signification, which requires two steps: first the meaning, then the concomitant.
3
Analyze the first objection and answer
The first objection claims that implicative signification involves transition (from whole to part), thus requiring two understandings. The answer by Abd al-Hakim distinguishes between priority in mental existence (part prior in itself) and priority in understanding from utterance (whole prior). They are compatible.
The objection mistakes self-priority for understanding-priority.
4
Analyze the second objection and answer
The second objection asserts that understanding the part twice (once as part of the whole, once as implicative signification) contradicts intuition. The answer simply denies the contradiction: understanding the part once is enough, so no double understanding occurs.
The objection is refuted by affirming that there is only one understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجزئه: تضمنا، وما لزم فهو التزام إن بعقل التزم. ولذلك قال بعضهم: الأحسن ما ذهب إليه بعض المحققين من أن دلالة التضمن فهم الجزء في ضمن الكل، ولا شك أنه إذا فهم المعنى فهمت أجزاؤه معه، فليس هناك إلا فهم واحد يسمى بالقياس إلى المعنى بتمامه: دلالة مطابقة، وبالقياس إلى جزئه: دلالة تضمن، وليس هناك انتقال من المعنى إلى جزئه، بخلاف دلالة الالتزام، فإنه لا بد فيها من الانتقال من المعنى إلى لازمه؛ ضرورة أن اللازم لا دخل له في الوضع أصلا. وأجيب عن الوجه الأول بما قاله عبد الحكيم: من أن اتفاقهم على تقدم الجزء في الوجود الذهني إنما هو من حيث فهم الجزء في ذاته، وهو لا ينافي تقدم الكل عليه من حيث فهمه من اللفظ، فيكون فهم الجزء من اللفظ متأخرا عن فهم الكل منه، وإن كان الجزء في ذاته متقدما على الكل. وعن الوجه الثاني: بمنع تكذيب الوجدان فهم الجزء مرتين، كما قاله بعض المحققين، فليتأمل. قوله: (وما لزم... إلخ) أي: ودلالة اللفظ على ما لزم فهو دلالة الالتزام، فهو معطوف على ما قبله. والفاء زائدة، وهذا أولى مما أشار له الشيخ الملوي من أن الفاء واقعة في جواب أما محذوفة، والتقدير: وأما ما لزم... إلخ، على أن المعنى: «وأما دلالة اللفظ على ما لزم... إلخ»؛ لأنه يصير الكلام عليه مستأنفا غير متعلق بما قبله، فيفوت حسن سبك التقسيم. وما واقعة على شيء لا على (لازم)، وإلا لضاع قوله: لزم، والإضافة في قولهم: «دلالة الالتزام من إضافة المسبب للسبب، وذكر الضمير في قوله: «فهو التزام»؛ رعاية للخبر. قوله: (إن بعقل التزم) أشار بهذا إلى: أنه يشترط في دلالة الالتزام أن يكون ذلك اللازم لازما
And his part: 'implicative', and what is necessary then it is 'necessary signification' if by intellect it is necessary. Therefore, some said: the best is what some of the verifiers went to: that implicative signification is the understanding of the part within the whole, and there is no doubt that when the meaning is understood, its parts are understood with it, so there is only one understanding called, in relation to the meaning in its entirety, 'correspondence signification', and in relation to its part, 'implicative signification', and there is no transition from the meaning to its part, unlike necessary signification, for it necessarily involves transition from the meaning to its necessary concomitant, because the necessary concomitant has no role in the linguistic convention at all. And it was answered regarding the first aspect by what Abd al-Hakim said: that their agreement on the priority of the part in mental existence is only from the perspective of understanding the part in itself, and that does not contradict the priority of the whole over it from the perspective of understanding it from the utterance, so the understanding of the part from the utterance is posterior to the understanding of the whole from it, even if the part in itself is prior to the whole. And regarding the second aspect: by denying the contradiction of intuition in understanding the part twice, as some of the verifiers said, so let it be considered. His saying: (and what is necessary... etc.) means: and the signification of the utterance on what is necessary is necessary signification, so it is conjoined to what preceded it. And the 'fa' is extra, and this is more appropriate than what Sheikh al-Mallawi indicated, that the 'fa' occurs in the answer of a hidden 'amma', and the estimation: 'and as for what is necessary... etc.', such that the meaning becomes: 'and as for the signification of the utterance on what is necessary... etc.' because the speech would become independent and not connected to what preceded it, thus the good composition of the division would be lost. And 'ma' applies to a thing not to 'necessary', otherwise his saying 'necessary' would be lost, and the idafa in their saying 'dilalat al-iltizam' is an idafa of the effect to the cause, and the pronoun was made masculine in his saying 'fahuwa iltizam' in observation of the predicate. His saying: (if by intellect it is necessary) indicated by this that it is required in necessary signification that that necessary concomitant be necessary
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage defines two types of signification: implicative (dalala tadammun) and necessary (dalala iltizam). The author begins with the matn line: 'And his part: implicative, and what is necessary then it is necessary signification if by intellect it is necessary.' The first part defines implicative signification as understanding the part within the whole, such that there is one understanding that is either correspondence (when considered as a whole) or implicative (when considered as a part). There is no transition from the whole to the part, unlike necessary signification which requires an intellectual transition from the meaning to its necessary concomitant, because the necessary concomitant is not part of the original linguistic convention. The author then addresses two objections. The first objection claimed that implicative signification involves a transition from whole to part, which would require two understandings. The answer, from Abd al-Hakim, distinguishes between the priority of the part in itself and the priority of the whole in understanding from the utterance. The part is prior in mental existence but the whole is prior in understanding from the utterance, so there is no contradiction. The second objection claimed that understanding the part twice (once as part of the whole and once as part of the signification) contradicts intuition. The answer simply denies that there is an intuitive contradiction, as some verifiers have said. Then the author explains the phrase 'and what is necessary...'. He clarifies grammatical points: the 'fa' is extra, and the phrase is conjoined to the previous. The preferred interpretation is that it is a continuation of the division, not an independent sentence. He also discusses the word 'ma' and the idafa. Finally, he notes that the condition 'if by intellect it is necessary' indicates that the necessary concomitant must be intellectually necessary, not merely customary or accidental. The passage uses examples such as 'human' (insan) being composed of 'animal' and 'rational', illustrating how understanding the whole precedes understanding the parts in auditory comprehension.

دلالة الالتزام

D) Neither requires transition.

Key Takeaway
Implicative signification is a single understanding of part within whole without transition; necessary signification requires intellectual transition to an external concomitant.

Three Views on Implicative Signification Transition
Author enumerates three opinions on implicative signification transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three scholarly views on whether dalāla al-taḍammun involves an intiqāl (transition) from whole to part, part to whole, or no transition at all, and explain why the third view is invalid.

Core Idea
The debate on implicative signification turns on the direction and existence of cognitive transition.

This passage from Bannani's commentary presents a crucial dispute among logicians: does understanding a whole word's meaning involve a mental transition to its parts? The answer determines how we analyse definitions and legal terms. Let's unpack the three views and see which one survives scrutiny.

Guided Path
1
List the three views
The passage presents three distinct positions on transition in implicative signification. Write them out in your own words: (1) transition from whole to part, (2) no transition at all, (3) transition from part to whole.
The first view is attributed to al-Fakhr al-Razi and others; the second to al-Amidi and Ibn al-Hajib; the third to al-Qutb.
2
Understand the direction of transition
For each view, identify the direction (if any) of the cognitive movement. In the first, the whole is understood first, then the part. In the second, there is one undivided understanding. In the third, the part is understood first, then the whole.
The third view reverses the order of the first.
3
Analyse the refutation of the third view
The passage declares the third view invalid. Follow the two reasons: (a) the word is not placed (wadʿ) for the part, so understanding the part from the word is not entailed; (b) even if the part were understood, the whole does not necessarily follow because the part is more general (aʿamm) than the whole (e.g., 'animal' is part of 'human' but understanding 'animal' does not entail 'human').
This is a logical refutation based on linguistic convention and the part-whole relationship.
4
Identify which view is strongest
Based on the refutation, the first two views remain possible. The passage does not explicitly favour one, but the refutation of the third is decisive. Consider which view aligns with how we actually understand terms in Islamic sciences.
Think about a fiqh example: 'ṣalāh' – do we first understand the whole prayer or its parts?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: أن فيها انتقالاً من فهم الكل إلى فهم الجزء، فيكون فهم الكل سابقاً، وفهم الجزء متأخراً عنه، وإليه ذهب الفخر، وابن التلمساني، والقرافي، وهو الذي في «المفتاح»، و«التلخيص»، و«جمع الجوامع»، وعليه السعد في المطول وشرح الشمسية. القول الثاني: أن دلالة التضمن لا انتقال فيها أصلاً، وليس للجزء فهم يخصه، وإنما هناك فهم واحد إن قيس إلى المجموع كان مطابقاً، وإن قيس إلى آحاد الأجزاء كان تضمناً، وإليه ذهب الآمدي، وابن الحاجب، والعضد، والسعد في حاشيته، والسيد في حاشيتي المطول وشرح المطالع، وابن أبي شريف. القول الثالث: أن للجزء فهماً من اللفظ يخصه، كما أن للكل فهماً يخصه، وأن فهم الجزء من اللفظ سابق على فهم الكل منه؛ هذا هو الذي دل عليه كلام القطب في شرح المطالع ومن تبعه، فيكون الانتقال عندهم من اللفظ إلى الجزء، ومن الجزء إلى الكل عكس القول الأول، وهذا القول باطل بالضرورة؛ إذ لا يلزم من إطلاق اللفظ فهم جزء المعنى؛ لعدم وضعه له، ولا من فهم الجزء فهم الكل؛ لأن الجزء أعم. اهـ (بناني)
The first: that in it there is a transition from understanding the whole to understanding the part, so understanding the whole is prior, and understanding the part is posterior. And this is the view of al-Fakhr, Ibn al-Tilmisani, and al-Qarafi, and it is what is in al-Miftah, al-Talkhis, and Jam' al-Jawami', and al-Sa'd follows it in al-Mutawwal and Sharh al-Shamsiyya. The second view: that implicative signification involves no transition at all, and there is no understanding specific to the part, rather there is one understanding: if it is measured against the whole it is correspondence, and if measured against the individual parts it is implicative. And this is the view of al-Amidi, Ibn al-Hajib, al-'Adud, al-Sa'd in his Hashiya, al-Sayyid in his two Hashiyas of al-Mutawwal and Sharh al-Matali', and Ibn Abi Sharif. The third view: that the part has an understanding from the utterance specific to it, just as the whole has an understanding specific to it, and that the understanding of the part from the utterance is prior to the understanding of the whole from it; this is what is indicated by the words of al-Qutb in Sharh al-Matali' and those who follow him. Thus, according to them, the transition is from the utterance to the part, and from the part to the whole, opposite to the first view. And this view is invalid by necessity, because from the utterance of the word, the understanding of a part of the meaning is not entailed, due to the lack of its placement for it, nor from understanding the part does understanding the whole follow, because the part is more general. End. (Bannani)
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage enumerates three scholarly opinions on the nature of implicative signification (dalala al-tadamman). The first opinion holds that implicative signification involves a transition from the whole to the part: first you understand the whole meaning, then you understand a part of it. This is attributed to al-Fakhr (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi), Ibn al-Tilmisani, and al-Qarafi, and is found in several key texts. The second opinion denies any transition; rather, there is a single understanding that can be considered either correspondence (if related to the whole) or implicative (if related to the parts). This is the view of al-Amidi, Ibn al-Hajib, al-'Adud, and others. The third opinion reverses the transition: first you understand the part from the utterance, then you understand the whole. This is attributed to al-Qutb and his followers. The passage then declares this third view invalid by necessity, because the utterance is not placed for the part, so understanding the part from it is not entailed, and even if the part is understood, understanding the whole does not follow because the part is more general (i.e., the whole is not a necessary consequence of the part). The passage uses no explicit examples, but the reasoning is purely logical. This shows the depth of disagreement among logicians on a fundamental point of semantics.

دلالة التضمن
فهم
مطابقة
وضع

A) The first view (whole to part transition)
B) The second view (no transition)
C) The third view (part to whole transition)
D) None of the views

Key Takeaway
The third view on implicative signification (part-to-whole transition) is invalid because the word is not placed for the part and understanding the part does not entail understanding the whole.

?
