Clear Concomitant in the More Specific Sense (Akhass)
Author defines lazim bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass and ghayr bayyin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and give an example of a clear necessary concomitant in the more specific sense (al-lāzim al-bayyin bi al-maʿnā al-akhaṣṣ) and distinguish it from the unclear concomitant (ghayr al-bayyin).

Core Idea
A clear concomitant in the specific sense is immediately known from the subject alone.

This lesson focuses on the first part of al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Bannani: the definition and criteria of the clear necessary concomitant in the more specific sense. We will also see what is excluded from this category, namely the unclear concomitant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject (malzum) and concomitant (lazim)
In the given definition, the subject is the term that implies something else. For example, 'four' is the subject and 'evenness' is the concomitant.
2
Check if merely conceiving the subject forces the conception of the concomitant
If thinking of the subject automatically brings the concomitant to mind (without any additional reasoning), then it is a clear concomitant in the more specific sense. Example: thinking of four as a number with two pairs immediately gives evenness.
3
If not immediate, check whether the relation is unclear
If understanding both terms does not give certainty of the relation without evidence, it is an unclear concomitant (ghayr bayyin). Example: origination (huduth) from the world requires a proof.
4
Distinguish the two types of clear concomitants
Note that this lesson covers only the specific sense. The more general sense will be covered separately. The key difference: in the specific sense, the concomitant is derived from the subject alone; in the general sense, both terms must be understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع الدلالة الوضعية (٢٥) وَجُزْنِهِ: «تَضَمُّناً»، وَمَا لَزِمْ فَهُوَ: «الْتِزَامُ إِنْ بِعَقْلِ الْتَزَمَ ذِهْنِيَّا، وَهُوَ المُسَمَّى بـ : «اللَّازِمِ البَيْنِ بِالمَعْنَى الأَخَصُ فِي اصْطَلَاحٍ بَعْضِ المَنَاطِقَةِ، وَضَابِطُهُ : أَنْ يَلْزَمَ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ المَلْزُومِ تَصَوُّرُ لَازِمِهِ؛ سَوَاءٌ : - كَانَ لَازِماً فِي الذِّهْنِ وَالخَارِجِ مَعاً كَـ : «الزَّوْجِيَّةِ» بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِـ: «الأَرْبَعَةِ» المُتَصَوَّرَةِ بِمَفْهُومِهَا المَخصُوصِ، وَهُوَ عَدَدٌ ذُو زَوْجَيْنِ . - أَوْ فِي الذِّهْنِ فَقَطْ كَـ: «البَصَرِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِـ: «العَمَى»، فَإِنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ العَمَى تَصَوُّرُ البَصَرِ، فَهُوَ لَازِمٌ فِي الذِّهْنِ، وَلَيْسَ لَازِما فِي الخَارِجِ، بَلْ مُنَافٍ. وَخَرَجَ بِهَذَا الشَّرْطِ : (1) اللَّازِمُ غَيْرُ البَيْنِ - أَيْ: غَيْرِ الوَاضِحِ -، وَضَابِطهُ : أَنْ لَا يَلْزَمَ مِنْ فَهْمِ المَلْزُومِ وَاللَّازِمِ الجَزْمُ بِاللُّزُومِ بَيْنَهُمَا، بَلْ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى الدَّلِيلِ كَـ: «الحُدُوثِ اللَّازِمِ لِـ : «العَالَمِ». (2) وَكَذَلِكَ اللَّازِمُ البَيِّنُ بِالمَعْنَى الأَعَم، وَضَابِطهُ : أَنْ يَلْزَمَ مِنْ فَهْمِ المَلْزُومِ وَاللَّازِم الجَزْمُ بِاللُّزُومِ بَيْنَهُمَا؛ سَوَاءٌ : - كَانَ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ المَلْزُومِ تَصَوُّرُ اللَّازِمِ كَـ : النُّوجِيَّةِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِـ : «الأَرْبَعَةِ». - أَوْ لَمْ يَلْزَمْ كَـ مُغَايَرَةِ الإِنْسَانِ لِلفَرَس مَثَلاً، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الإِنْسَانِ تَصَوُّرُ المُغَايَرَةِ المَذْكُورَةِ، لَكِنْ إِذَا فُهِمَ الإِنْسَانُ وَفُهِمَتِ المُغَايَرَةُ المَذْكُورَةُ جُزِمَ بِاللُّزُومِ بَيْنَهُمَا. فَتَحَصَّلَ أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى : بَيِّنٍ، وَغَيْرِ بَيْنِ ؛ وَالأَوَّلُ يَنْقَسِمُ : إِلَى لَازِمٍ بَيِّنِ بِالمَعْنَى الْأَخَصَّ، وَإِلَى لَازِم بَيِّنِ بِالمَعْنَى الأَعَم ؛ وَوَجْهُ تَسْمِيَتِهِمَا بِذَلِكَ : أَنَّ الأَوَّلَ فَرْدٌ مِنَ الثَّانِي فَهُوَ أَخَصُّ مِنْهُ، وَهَذِهِ إِحْدَى طَرِيقَتَيْنِ فِي التَّقْسِيمِ. ثَانِيَتُهُمَا - وَهِيَ غَيْرُ مُنَافِيَةٍ لِلأُوْلَى - : أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ يَنْقَسِمُ : (۱) - إِلَى لَازِمِ فِي الذِّهْنِ وَالخَارِجِ مَعَاً
Types of conventional indication. (25) And its part by 'tadhamun' (inclusion), and what is necessary is 'iltizam' (implication) if by mind it is necessary, and it is called 'the clear necessary concomitant in the more specific sense' in the terminology of some logicians. Its criterion: that the conception of the subject necessitates the conception of its concomitant; whether it is necessary in both mind and external together, like 'evenness' in relation to 'four' conceived in its specific meaning, which is a number having two pairs; or only in the mind, like 'sight' in relation to 'blindness', for the conception of blindness necessitates the conception of sight, so it is necessary in the mind but not in the external, rather it is contradictory. By this condition are excluded: (1) The unclear necessary concomitant – i.e., unclear – and its criterion: that from understanding the subject and the concomitant, certainty of the relation between them does not follow, but rather depends on evidence, like 'origination' necessary for 'the world'. (2) And likewise the clear necessary concomitant in the more general sense, and its criterion: that from understanding the subject and the concomitant, certainty of the relation between them follows; whether it follows from the conception of the subject the conception of the concomitant, like 'evenness' in relation to 'four'; or does not follow, like the difference of human from horse, for example, for the conception of human does not necessitate the conception of that difference, but when human is understood and that difference is understood, one is certain of the relation between them. Hence it is concluded that the necessary concomitant divides into clear and unclear; and the first divides into a clear necessary concomitant in the more specific sense and a clear necessary concomitant in the more general sense; and the reason for naming them thus is that the first is an instance of the second, so it is more specific than it. This is one of two methods of division. The second method – and it is not inconsistent with the first – is that the necessary concomitant divides into: (1) necessary in both mind and external together.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the different types of logical implication (iltizam) under conventional indication (dalala wad'iyya). The author begins with the matn line that states: 'And its part is tadhamun (inclusion), and what is necessary is iltizam (implication) if it is so by a mental commitment.' This sets the stage: a word may indicate its whole meaning (mutabaqa), a part of its meaning (tadhamun), or something external that necessarily accompanies the meaning (iltizam). The focus here is on iltizam.

The author then clarifies that the 'necessary concomitant' (lazim) under iltizam must be such that merely conceiving the subject (malzum) forces the conception of the concomitant. This is called 'al-lazim al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass' (the clear necessary concomitant in the more specific sense) in the terminology of some logicians. Two examples are given: (1) Evenness (zawjiyya) in relation to the number four – when you think of 'four' as a number having two pairs, evenness is immediately conceived. This concomitant exists both in the mind and in reality. (2) Sight (basar) in relation to blindness ('ama) – when you conceive blindness, which is the absence of sight, you necessarily conceive sight. But this relation is only mental, not external, because in reality blindness and sight are mutually exclusive. The author then notes that this condition excludes two kinds of lazims.

First excluded is the 'lazim ghayr al-bayyin' (unclear concomitant). Its criterion: understanding both subject and concomitant does not immediately give certainty of the relation; evidence is needed. Example: 'origination' (huduth) in relation to 'the world'. Knowing the world does not automatically make you certain that it is originated; you need a proof.

Second excluded is the 'lazim bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'am' (clear concomitant in the more general sense). Its criterion: understanding both terms gives certainty of the relation, but the concomitant may not be directly conceived from the subject. Two sub-cases: (a) It follows from conceiving the subject, like evenness for four – same as the more specific sense. (b) It does not follow from the mere conception of the subject, but once both are understood, the relation is certain. Example: the difference (muhayara) between human and horse. Thinking of 'human' does not necessarily bring to mind 'difference from horse', but if you consider both meanings, you immediately know they are different.

The passage then summarizes that the necessary concomitant (lazim) divides into clear (bayyin) and unclear (ghayr bayyin). The clear further divides into two: clear in the more specific sense (where merely conceiving the subject gives the concomitant) and clear in the more general sense (where understanding both gives certainty). The more specific is a subset of the more general.

Finally, a second method of division is introduced but only partially given: dividing lazim into those that are necessary in both mind and external together. This second method is complementary to the first. (The remainder of this second method appears in subsequent pages.)

Key Terms
اللازم
اللازم البين بالمعنى الأخص
اللازم غير البين

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The relationship between 'world' and 'origination'
B) The relationship between 'four' and 'evenness'
C) The relationship between 'human' and 'different from horse'
D) The relationship between 'blindness' and 'sight'

Key Takeaway
A clear concomitant in the more specific sense is such that the subject alone forces the conception of the concomitant, making it self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Clear Concomitant in the More General Sense (A’am)
Author defines lazim bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'am and summarizes the divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and give an example of a clear necessary concomitant in the more general sense (al-lāzim al-bayyin bi al-maʿnā al-āʿamm) and explain how it differs from the more specific sense.

Core Idea
A clear concomitant in the general sense is known with certainty once both terms are understood, even if not immediate from the subject alone.

This lesson completes the classification of clear concomitants by examining the more general sense. Here, the relation is certain but not necessarily immediate from the subject; the concomitant may only become evident when both subject and concomitant are considered together.

Guided Path
1
Understand the criterion of the more general sense
The criterion: from understanding both the subject and the concomitant, one is certain of the relation between them. This does not require that the concomitant be conceived from the subject alone.
2
Compare two sub-cases
The more general sense includes two possibilities: (a) the concomitant is immediate from the subject (like evenness from four) – this coincides with the more specific sense; (b) the concomitant is not immediate, but once both are understood, the relation is certain (like the difference between human and horse).
3
Distinguish from the unclear concomitant
If understanding both terms does not yield certainty but requires external proof, it is unclear (ghayr bayyin). Example: origination from the world.
4
Apply to real examples from Islamic sciences
In Kalam, the relationship between 'contingent' and 'needs a cause' is often considered a clear concomitant in the more general sense: once you understand both, you are certain of the link, but it may not be immediate from the term 'contingent' alone.

(2) وَكَذَلِكَ اللَّازِمُ البَيِّنُ بِالمَعْنَى الأَعَم، وَضَابِطهُ : أَنْ يَلْزَمَ مِنْ فَهْمِ المَلْزُومِ وَاللَّازِم الجَزْمُ بِاللُّزُومِ بَيْنَهُمَا؛ سَوَاءٌ : - كَانَ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ المَلْزُومِ تَصَوُّرُ اللَّازِمِ كَـ : النُّوجِيَّةِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِـ : «الأَرْبَعَةِ». - أَوْ لَمْ يَلْزَمْ كَـ مُغَايَرَةِ الإِنْسَانِ لِلفَرَس مَثَلاً، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الإِنْسَانِ تَصَوُّرُ المُغَايَرَةِ المَذْكُورَةِ، لَكِنْ إِذَا فُهِمَ الإِنْسَانُ وَفُهِمَتِ المُغَايَرَةُ المَذْكُورَةُ جُزِمَ بِاللُّزُومِ بَيْنَهُمَا. فَتَحَصَّلَ أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى : بَيِّنٍ، وَغَيْرِ بَيْنِ ؛ وَالأَوَّلُ يَنْقَسِمُ : إِلَى لَازِمٍ بَيِّنِ بِالمَعْنَى الْأَخَصَّ، وَإِلَى لَازِم بَيِّنِ بِالمَعْنَى الأَعَم ؛ وَوَجْهُ تَسْمِيَتِهِمَا بِذَلِكَ : أَنَّ الأَوَّلَ فَرْدٌ مِنَ الثَّانِي فَهُوَ أَخَصُّ مِنْهُ، وَهَذِهِ إِحْدَى طَرِيقَتَيْنِ فِي التَّقْسِيمِ. ثَانِيَتُهُمَا - وَهِيَ غَيْرُ مُنَافِيَةٍ لِلأُوْلَى - : أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ يَنْقَسِمُ : (۱) - إِلَى لَازِمِ فِي الذِّهْنِ وَالخَارِجِ مَعَاً
Types of conventional indication. (25) And its part by 'tadhamun' (inclusion), and what is necessary is 'iltizam' (implication) if by mind it is necessary, and it is called 'the clear necessary concomitant in the more specific sense' in the terminology of some logicians. Its criterion: that the conception of the subject necessitates the conception of its concomitant; whether it is necessary in both mind and external together, like 'evenness' in relation to 'four' conceived in its specific meaning, which is a number having two pairs; or only in the mind, like 'sight' in relation to 'blindness', for the conception of blindness necessitates the conception of sight, so it is necessary in the mind but not in the external, rather it is contradictory. By this condition are excluded: (1) The unclear necessary concomitant – i.e., unclear – and its criterion: that from understanding the subject and the concomitant, certainty of the relation between them does not follow, but rather depends on evidence, like 'origination' necessary for 'the world'. (2) And likewise the clear necessary concomitant in the more general sense, and its criterion: that from understanding the subject and the concomitant, certainty of the relation between them follows; whether it follows from the conception of the subject the conception of the concomitant, like 'evenness' in relation to 'four'; or does not follow, like the difference of human from horse, for example, for the conception of human does not necessitate the conception of that difference, but when human is understood and that difference is understood, one is certain of the relation between them. Hence it is concluded that the necessary concomitant divides into clear and unclear; and the first divides into a clear necessary concomitant in the more specific sense and a clear necessary concomitant in the more general sense; and the reason for naming them thus is that the first is an instance of the second, so it is more specific than it. This is one of two methods of division. The second method – and it is not inconsistent with the first – is that the necessary concomitant divides into: (1) necessary in both mind and external together.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the different types of logical implication (iltizam) under conventional indication (dalala wad'iyya). The author begins with the matn line that states: 'And its part is tadhamun (inclusion), and what is necessary is iltizam (implication) if it is so by a mental commitment.' This sets the stage: a word may indicate its whole meaning (mutabaqa), a part of its meaning (tadhamun), or something external that necessarily accompanies the meaning (iltizam). The focus here is on iltizam.

The author then clarifies that the 'necessary concomitant' (lazim) under iltizam must be such that merely conceiving the subject (malzum) forces the conception of the concomitant. This is called 'al-lazim al-bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass' (the clear necessary concomitant in the more specific sense) in the terminology of some logicians. Two examples are given: (1) Evenness (zawjiyya) in relation to the number four – when you think of 'four' as a number having two pairs, evenness is immediately conceived. This concomitant exists both in the mind and in reality. (2) Sight (basar) in relation to blindness ('ama) – when you conceive blindness, which is the absence of sight, you necessarily conceive sight. But this relation is only mental, not external, because in reality blindness and sight are mutually exclusive. The author then notes that this condition excludes two kinds of lazims.

First excluded is the 'lazim ghayr al-bayyin' (unclear concomitant). Its criterion: understanding both subject and concomitant does not immediately give certainty of the relation; evidence is needed. Example: 'origination' (huduth) in relation to 'the world'. Knowing the world does not automatically make you certain that it is originated; you need a proof.

Second excluded is the 'lazim bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'am' (clear concomitant in the more general sense). Its criterion: understanding both terms gives certainty of the relation, but the concomitant may not be directly conceived from the subject. Two sub-cases: (a) It follows from conceiving the subject, like evenness for four – same as the more specific sense. (b) It does not follow from the mere conception of the subject, but once both are understood, the relation is certain. Example: the difference (muhayara) between human and horse. Thinking of 'human' does not necessarily bring to mind 'difference from horse', but if you consider both meanings, you immediately know they are different.

The passage then summarizes that the necessary concomitant (lazim) divides into clear (bayyin) and unclear (ghayr bayyin). The clear further divides into two: clear in the more specific sense (where merely conceiving the subject gives the concomitant) and clear in the more general sense (where understanding both gives certainty). The more specific is a subset of the more general.

Finally, a second method of division is introduced but only partially given: dividing lazim into those that are necessary in both mind and external together. This second method is complementary to the first. (The remainder of this second method appears in subsequent pages.)

Key Terms
اللازم البين بالمعنى الأعم
المغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a clear necessary concomitant in the more general sense (al-lāzim al-bayyin bi al-maʿnā al-āʿamm)?
A) The relation between 'world' and 'origination'
B) The relation between 'four' and 'evenness'
C) The relation between 'human' and 'different from horse'
D) The relation between 'blindness' and 'sight'

Key Takeaway
A clear concomitant in the more general sense yields certainty when both subject and concomitant are understood, even if the concomitant is not immediate from the subject alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In Kalam, the statement 'The world is contingent (hadith); everything contingent has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' The relationship between 'contingent' and 'has a cause' is considered a clear concomitant in the more general sense. Explain why it is not in the more specific sense.

Classification of the Necessary Concomitant (Lazim)
Author defines and classifies the necessary concomitant into clear and unclear, then by location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between clear and non-clear necessary concomitants, and identify the two methods of dividing lazim.

Core Idea
The necessary concomitant is classified by clarity and by mode of existence, revealing its logical and epistemological role.

In Islamic logic, the necessary concomitant (al-lazim) is a concept that necessarily follows from another. This lesson explores its classification into clear (bayyin) and non-clear (ghayr bayyin), and further into subtypes based on clarity and existential mode. Understanding these divisions is crucial for analyzing connotational implications (dalala iltizamiyya).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions of lazim
Look at the passage: after discussing the non-clear and clear in the general sense, the author says 'It emerges that the necessary concomitant divides into: clear and non-clear'. Then he presents another division. So there are two divisions: one by clarity, another by mode of existence.
2
Distinguish clear vs. non-clear by criterion
The non-clear (ghayr al-bayyin) requires evidence for the necessity; the clear (bayyin) yields immediate definite judgment of necessity upon understanding both terms. The passage explicitly gives criteria: non-clear requires evidence; clear does not.
3
Subdivide clear into more specific and more general
Clear lazim is further divided: more specific (akhass) where conceptualizing the subject alone gives the concomitant (e.g., fourness from four); more general (a'amm) where the necessity becomes clear only after both terms are understood (e.g., difference between man and horse). The passage explains the naming: the more specific is a subset of the more general.
4
Understand the second division by mode of existence
The second division categorizes lazim into: (1) in both mind and external (e.g., courage of lion), (2) in mind only (e.g., sight for blindness), (3) in external only (e.g., blackness for crow). This is not contradictory to the first division.
5
Recognize the scholarly disagreement on the sufficient condition
The passage states that the preponderant opinion requires the clear in the more specific sense for a valid lazim, but Fakhr al-Razi and many later scholars accept the more general sense. This disagreement affects which implications are considered necessary.

وَخَرَجَ بِهَذَا الشَّرْطِ : اللَّازِمُ غَيْرُ البَيْنِ - أَيْ: غَيْرِ الوَاضِحِ ) ، وَضَابِطهُ : أَنْ لَا يَلْزَمَ مِنْ فَهْمِ المَلْزُومِ وَاللَّازِمِ الجَزْمُ بِاللُّزُومِ بَيْنَهُمَا ، بَلْ يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى الدَّلِيلِ . (۲) - وَكَذَلِكَ اللَّازِمُ البَيِّنُ بِالمَعْنَى الأَعَم (۳) ، وَضَابِطهُ : أَنْ يَلْزَمَ مِنْ فَهْمِ المَلْزُومِ وَاللَّازِم الجَزْمُ بِاللُّزُومِ بَيْنَهُمَا ؛ سَوَاءٌ : - كَانَ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ المَلْزُومِ تَصَوُّرُ اللَّازِمِ كَـ : النُّوجِيَّةِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِـ : «الأَرْبَعَةِ» . گ - أَوْ لَمْ يَلْزَمْ كَـ مُغَايَرَةِ الإِنْسَانِ لِلفَرَس مَثَلاً )) ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الإِنْسَانِ تَصَوُّرُ المُغَايَرَةِ المَذْكُورَةِ، لَكِنْ إِذَا فُهِمَ الإِنْسَانُ وَفُهِمَتِ المُغَايَرَةُ المَذْكُورَةُ جُزِمَ بِاللُّزُومِ بَيْنَهُمَا . فَتَحَصَّلَ أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى : بَيِّنٍ، وَغَيْرِ بَيْنِ ; وَالأَوَّلُ يَنْقَسِمُ : إِلَى لَازِمٍ بَيِّنِ بِالمَعْنَى الْأَخَصَّ، وَإِلَى لازم بَيِّنِ بِالمَعْنَى الأَعَم ; وَوَجْهُ تَسْمِيَتِهِمَا بِذَلِكَ : أَنَّ الأَوَّلَ فَرْدٌ مِنَ الثَّانِي فَهُوَ أَخَصُّ مِنْهُ، وَهَذِهِ إِحْدَى طَرِيقَتَيْنِ فِي التَّقْسِيمِ . ثَانِيَتُهُمَا - وَهِيَ غَيْرُ مُنَافِيَةٍ لِلأُوْلَى - : أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ يَنْقَسِمُ : إِلَى لَازِمِ فِي الذِّهْنِ وَالخَارِجِ مَعَاً ; كَـ : الشَّجَاعَةِ لِلأَسَدِ. وَإِلَى لَازِمِ فِي الذِّهْنِ فَقَطْ ; كَـ : البَصَرِ لِلعَمَى. وَإِلَى لَازِمٍ فِي الخَارِجِ فَقَطْ كَـ: السَّوَادِ لِلْغُرَابِ. وَمَا تَقَدَّمَ مِنِ اشْتِرَاطِ اللَّازِمِ البَيِّنِ بِالمَعْنَى الأَخَصُ هُوَ الرَّاجِحُ ، وَذَهَبَ الفَخْرُ كَكَثِيرٍ مِنَ المُتَأخّرِينَ إِلَى أَنَّهُ يَكْفِي اللَّازِمُ البَيِّنُ بِالمَعْنَى الأَعَمِّ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ . قوله : (كَ الشَّجَاعَةِ لِلأَسَدِ) قد يمنع كون شجاعة الأسد مِنَ اللَّازم الذهني المرادف للبين بالمعنى الأخص ; لإمكان تصور الأسد» مع الغفلة عن شجاعته; إلَّا أن يمنع، فتأمل .
And excluded by this condition is: the non-clear necessary concomitant – meaning the unclear – its criterion is that from understanding the subject and the concomitant there does not follow a definite judgment of necessity between them, but rather it depends on evidence. (2) And likewise the clear necessary concomitant in the more general sense, its criterion is that from understanding the subject and the concomitant there follows a definite judgment of necessity between them; whether it follows from the conceptualization of the subject the conceptualization of the concomitant, like the 'fourness' relative to 'four', or it does not follow, like the difference between man and horse for example, for the conceptualization of man does not necessitate the conceptualization of that difference, but if man is understood and that difference is understood, then necessity between them is judged. It emerges that the necessary concomitant divides into: clear and non-clear; and the first divides into: a clear concomitant in the more specific sense, and a clear concomitant in the more general sense; and the reason for naming them thus is that the first is an individual of the second, so it is more specific than it. This is one of two ways in the division. The second – and it is not contradictory to the first – is that the necessary concomitant divides into: a concomitant in the mind and in the external together, like courage for the lion; and a concomitant in the mind only, like sight for blindness; and a concomitant in the external only, like blackness for the crow. And what preceded from the condition of the clear concomitant in the more specific sense is the preponderant opinion, and Fakhr [al-Razi] along with many of the later scholars held that the clear concomitant in the more general sense suffices, as previously mentioned. His statement: ('like courage for the lion') it may be disputed that courage of the lion is from the mental concomitant synonymous with the clear in the more specific sense, because it is possible to conceptualize the lion while heedless of its courage, unless it is prevented, so reflect.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the necessary concomitant (al-lāzim) within the context of implication (dalāla). The author begins by stating what is excluded by the condition for a valid necessary concomitant: the non-clear (ghayr al-bayyin) concomitant, which is unclear and requires evidence to establish the necessity. Its criterion is that understanding both subject and concomitant does not immediately produce a definite judgment of necessity; proof is needed, e.g., the temporality of the world. Then the author discusses the clear concomitant in the more general sense (al-bayyin bi al-ma’nā al-a‘amm): its criterion is that understanding both terms yields a definite judgment of necessity, regardless of whether conceptualization of the subject itself necessitates the concomitant. Example: conceptualizing 'four' does not immediately give the concept of 'fourness', but once both are understood, the necessity is clear. Another example: the difference between man and horse – understanding man does not give the concept of difference, but if both are understood, the necessity is judged. From this, we see the first division: lazim divides into bayyin and ghayr bayyin. Bayyin itself subdivides into bayyin bi al-ma‘nā al-akhass (more specific) and bayyin bi al-ma‘nā al-a‘amm (more general). The more specific is a subset of the more general, hence the naming. This is one method. Then the author presents a second method, not contradictory: lazim divides by mode of existence: (1) in both mind and external (like courage of the lion – courage is mental concept but also realized externally), (2) in mind only (like sight for blindness – blindness is mental concept, the concomitant 'sight' is only mental relative to blindness), (3) in external only (like blackness for the crow – blackness exists externally). The passage concludes with the preponderant opinion that the condition for a valid lazim is the clear in the more specific sense, contrary to Fakhr al-Din al-Razi and many later scholars who accept the more general sense. A marginal note raises a doubt: whether 'courage for the lion' truly qualifies as clear in the more specific sense, since one can conceive of a lion without thinking of its courage. This highlights the subtlety of the condition.

Key Terms
اللازم
البين
غير البين
لازم في الذهن

A) The non-clear (ghayr al-bayyin)
B) The clear in the more general sense (bayyin bi al-ma‘nā al-a‘amm)
C) The clear in the more specific sense (bayyin bi al-ma‘nā al-akhass)
D) The concomitant in the external only

Key Takeaway
The necessary concomitant (lazim) is classified first by the immediacy of the necessity (clear vs. non-clear), then by the mode of existence (mind and/or external), and the clear itself is subdivided into more specific and more general senses.

The Twofold Division of Meaningful Expressions
Bajuri defines the division of expressions into compound and single.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any meaningful Arabic expression as either murakkab or mufrad based on whether its parts indicate part of its meaning.

Core Idea
A word is mufrad if its parts carry no independent meaning; murakkab if they do.

Before we can analyze propositions and arguments, we must first understand the building blocks: words. In Islamic logic, every meaningful expression (musta'mal) falls into one of two categories: murakkab (compound) or mufrad (single). This division is foundational for defining terms and constructing definitions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scope
Only musta'mal (meaningful) expressions are divided; muhmal (meaningless) is excluded. Observe how Bajuri explicitly excludes al-muhmal.
The exclusion is important because meaningless words have no part-indication.
2
Check the part-indication criterion
For murakkab, a part of the word indicates part of its meaning (e.g., 'Abd' in 'Abdullah' indicates servant). For mufrad, no part does (e.g., 'Zayd').
The criterion is from the commentary, not the matn.
3
Test with Islamic examples
Classify 'Bismillah' as compound (Bism + Allah), 'Kitab' as single (letters don't mean book individually).
Use terms from Quran and fiqh.
4
Apply to logical definitions
In a definition, the definiendum is often mufrad, but the definition itself (hadd) is a murakkab phrase. This helps distinguish word level from phrase level.

Source Text

(فَصْلٌ فِي مَبَاحِثِ الْأَلْفَاظِ) (٢٦) مُسْتَعْمَلُ الأَلْفَاظِ حَيْثُ يُوجَدُ : إِمَّا مُرَكَّبٌ ، وَإِمَّا مُفْرَدُ. (فَصْلٌ فِي مَبَاحِثِ الْأَلْفَاظِ) [ قَوْلُهُ : (فَصْلٌ فِي مَبَاحِثِ الأَلْفَاظِ) أَيْ : فِي المَسَائِلِ الَّتِي يُبْحَثُ فِيهَا عَنِ الْأَلْفَاظِ مِنْ حَيْثُ الإِفْرَادِ وَالتَّرْكِيبِ، وَمَا يُلائِمُهُمَا كَالكُلِّيَّةِ والجُزْيَّة . فَ المَبَاحِثُ بِمَعْنَى : المَسَائِلِ المَذْكُورَةِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا جَمْعُ : مَبْحَتْ بِمَعْنَى : مَكَانِ البَحْثِ، وَهُوَ فِي الأَصْلِ : التَّفْتِيسُ عَنْ بَاطِنِ الشَّيْءٍ حِسَّا»، ثُمَّ اسْتُعْمِلَ عُرْفاً فِي بَيَانِ الشَّيْءِ وَالكَشْفِ عَنْهُ ، فَقَوْلُهُمْ: مَبْحَثُ كَذَا بِمَعْنَى : مَكَانِ بَيَانِهِ وَالكَشْفِ عَنْهُ ، وَذَلِكَ المَكَانُ كِنَايَةٌ عَنِ المَسَائِلِ الَّتِي يُبْحَثُ فِيهَا عَنْهُ. وَبِقَوْلِنَا : مِنْ حَيْثُ ... إلخ» انْدَفَعَ مَا قَدْ يُقَالُ : كَلَامُهُ يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ مَبْحَثَ الدَّلَالَاتِ لَيْسَ مِنْ مَبَاحِثِ الأَلْفَاظِ ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ، فَتَأَمَّلْ. قَوْلُهُ : (مُسْتَعْمَلُ الأَلْفَاظِ ... إلخ) أي : المُسْتَعْمَلُ مِنْهَا، فَالْإِضَافَةُ عَلَى مَعْنَى مِنْ». وَخَرَجَ عَنْ ذَلِكَ : المُهْمَلُ، فَلَا يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى ذَلِكَ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا مَعْنَى لَهُ حَتَّى يُقَالَ فِيهِ : «المُرَكَّبُ : مَا دَلَ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ، وَالمُفْرَدُ : مَا لَا يَدُلُّ جُزْؤُهُ ... إلخ » .
(Chapter on discussions of words) (26) The expressions in use, wherever found, are either compound or single. (Chapter on discussions of words) [His saying: 'Chapter on discussions of words' means: concerning the topics in which words are investigated from the aspect of singleness and composition, and what is suitable to them such as universality and particularity. So 'mabāḥith' means the mentioned topics; because it is the plural of 'mabḥath' meaning place of investigation, which originally is: searching for the interior of a thing physically, then it was used conventionally for the exposition of a thing and uncovering it. So their saying: 'mabḥath of such-and-such' means: the place of its exposition and uncovering, and that place is a metaphor for the topics in which it is investigated. And by our saying 'from the aspect... etc.' it is refuted what might be said: his speech necessitates that the topic of implications is not from the discussions of words, and it is not so, so ponder. His saying: 'The expressions in use... etc.' means: those which are used, so the annexation is in the sense of 'from'. And excluded from that is the meaningless (al-muhmal), so it does not divide into that because it has no meaning such that it could be said about it: 'The compound is what part of it indicates part of its meaning, and the single is what its part does not indicate... etc.']
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory passage introducing the chapter on words (mabahith al-alfaz). Bajuri explains the meaning of 'mabāhith' (discussions) as topics or places of investigation, originally referring to physical search but conventionally used for conceptual exposition. He then clarifies the main division: expressions in actual use (musta'mal) are either compound (murakkab) or single (mufrad). The compound is defined (in the commentary) as that whose part indicates part of its meaning, while the single is that whose part does not indicate part of its meaning. Exclusion of the meaningless (muhmal) is noted: such words have no meaning and thus cannot be so divided. The passage also addresses a potential objection: that the earlier discussion of implications (dalalat) might not belong to this chapter, but Bajuri asserts it does, as it concerns words from the aspect of meaning relations. Throughout, the commentary follows the author's order: first defining the scope, then defining the terms, then excluding irrelevant cases, and defending the inclusion of previous material.

Key Terms
مُسْتَعْمَل
مُرَكَّب
مُفْرَد
مُهْمَل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Bajuri, what is excluded from the division of expressions into compound and single?
A) The ambiguous (mujmal)
B) The metaphorical (majaz)
C) The meaningless (muhmal)
D) The general ('amm)

Key Takeaway
The first step in logical analysis is to identify whether an expression is simple or compound, based on whether its parts carry meaning.

Understanding the Scope of Mabahith al-Alfaz
Bajuri explains the scope of the chapter on words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the topic of implications (dalalat) is included in the discussions of words and define the term 'mabahith' as used in logic.

Core Idea
'Mabahith' means topics of investigation, and the chapter on words includes both words and their implications.

After learning the basic division, Bajuri clarifies what he means by 'discussions of words'. He defends that the earlier topic of implication (dalalat) rightly belongs here, and explains the semantic shift of the term 'mabahith' from physical search to conceptual exposition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the literal meaning of mabahith
Originally, mabahith (singular mabḥath) means a place of physical search. Bajuri notes this root to highlight the shift in usage.
2
Recognize the conventional usage in logic
In logic texts, mabahith conventionally means 'topics' or 'questions' under investigation, not physical places.
This is a common metaphor.
3
Note the inclusion of dalalat
Bajuri refutes an objection that the topic of implications (dalalat) might be separate. He asserts it belongs because words are studied not only in themselves but also in their signifying function.
The passage says: 'wa laysa kadhalik' – it is not so.
4
Apply the scope to other science texts
When you open a logic text, look for the chapter on words (alfaz) and check whether it includes matters of definition and implication. This helps you navigate the work.

Defining the Compound Term: Primary Intended Indication
Author defines compound term and answers objections that the definition includes non-compounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given expression is a compound (murakkab) according to the precise definition and evaluate objections to its exclusivity.

Core Idea
A compound term requires primary intended indication of its parts, not mere composition.

This lesson examines the definition of compound (murakkab) as one of the five universals. The author refines the basic definition—'whose part indicates a part of its meaning'—by adding the condition that the indication must be primarily intended. We will explore the original definition, the objections raised against it, and the nuanced responses that clarify the boundary between compound and simple terms.

Guided Path
1
Extract the core definition
Locate the definiendum 'murakkab' and the defining phrase: 'that whose part indicates a part of its meaning in the opposite way of what follows.' Identify the key components: the term, its parts, and the indication of meaning.
The 'opposite way' refers to the definition of simple (mufrad) that follows.
2
Apply the definition to counterexamples
Test the definition on the examples given: 'Abdullah', 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq', and 'Hujjat al-Islam' as a proper name. Determine whether each satisfies the literal definition.
These are brought as objections to show the definition is not exclusive (ghayr māniʿ).
3
Understand the first answer: primary intention
The author answers that the definition implicitly requires the part's indication to be primarily intended (dalāla maqsūda bil-asāla). In the examples, the indication is either unintended or only secondary.
This preserves the definition's exclusivity by adding a hidden condition.
4
Analyze al-Mallawi's alternative answer
Al-Mallawi argues that when 'Abdullah' is used as a proper name, the parts no longer indicate at all; thus, they are excluded from the definition. For 'Hujjat al-Islam', he considers it a compound, so it should be included, not excluded.
This approach treats proper names as losing their compositional meaning entirely.
5
Synthesize the two perspectives
Some investigators reconcile by noting that a term can be simple when considered as a proper name and compound when considered for its literal meaning. Evaluate which answer best resolves the objection.
This shows the definition's depth and the importance of intention in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَأَوَّلٌ : مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزُءِ مَعْنَاهُ بِعَكْسِ مَا تَلَا. قَوْلُهُ : (مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ ... إلخ) أي : الَّذِي أَوْ لَفْظُ دَلَّ ... إلخ، وَ مَا : مَوْصُولَةٌ، أَوْ مَوْصُوفَةٌ . وَخَرَجَ بِقَوْلِهِ : مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ مَا لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ بِأَنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ جُزْءٌ أَصْلاً : بَاءِ الجَرِ وَلَامِهِ، أَوْ لَهُ جُزْءٌ لَكِنْ لَا يَدُلُّ كَـ : «زَيْدِ». وَاعْتُرِضَ عَلَى المُصَنَّفِ : بِأَنَّ هَذَا التَّعْرِيفَ غَيْرُ مَانِعِ ؛ لِشُمُولِهِ نَحْوَ : «عَبْدُ اللَّهِ» وَ: «الحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ وَ : «حُجَّةُ الإِسْلَامِ عَلَماً، مَعَ قَصْدِ الوَاضِعِ فِي الأَخِيرِ أَنَّ المُسَمَّى حُجَّةٌ فِي الدِّيْنِ. وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُرَادَ مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ دَلَالَةٌ مَقْصُودَةٌ بِالأَصَالَةِ، وَلَا كَذَلِكَ الدَّلَالَةُ فِيمَا ذُكِرَ؛ لِأَنَّهَا فِيمَا عَدَا الأَخِيرِ غَيْرُ مَقْصُودَةٍ، وَفِي الأَخِيرِ غَيْرُ مَقْصُودَةِ بِالأَصَالَةِ، بَلْ بِالتَّبعِ. وَأَجَابَ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ : بِأَنَّ مَا عَدَا الأَخِيرِ لَا يَدُلُّ جُزْؤُهُ حَالَ العَلَمِيَّةِ، فَهُوَ خَارِجُ بِقَوْلِهِ : «دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ، وَأَمَّا مَا يُتَوَهَّمُ مِنْ دَلَالَتِهِ فَإِنَّمَا هُوَ قَبْلَ العَلَمِيَّةِ، وَاخْتَارَ أَنَّ الأَخِيرَ مُرَكَّبٌ لَا مُفْرَدٌ، فَلَا يَصِحُ إِخْرَاجُهُ لِوُجُوبِ إِدْخَالِهِ حِينَئِذٍ، وَاخْتَارَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ أَنَّهُ مُفْرَدٌ بِاعْتِبَارِ قَصْدِ الوَاضِعِ المَعْنَى العَلَمِيِّ، وَمُرَكَّبٌ بِاعْتِبَارِ قَصْدِهِ المَعْنَى التَّرْكِيبِيِّ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ .
So the first [is]: that whose part indicates a part of its meaning in the opposite way of what follows. His statement: 'whose part indicates...' etc. means: that which, or a word, indicates... etc., and 'ma' is a relative pronoun or described noun. By his statement 'whose part indicates' there exits what is not like that, i.e., either it has no part at all like 'ba' of jar and 'lam', or has a part but it does not indicate like 'Zayd'. An objection was made against the author: that this definition is not exclusive because it covers things like 'Abdullah', 'the rational animal', and 'Hujjat al-Islam' as a proper name, with the intention of the coiner in the last that the named is a proof in religion. The answer: What is meant is that its part indicates with an intended primary indication, and the indication in the mentioned examples is not like that; because in other than the last it is not intended, and in the last it is not intended primarily but rather secondarily. Al-Mallawi answered: that other than the last does not have its part indicate when used as a proper name, so it is excluded by his statement 'its part indicates'. As for what is imagined of its indication, that is only before being a proper name. He chose that the last is a compound not a simple, so its exclusion is not valid because it must be included then. And some investigators chose that it is a simple considering the coiner's intention of the proper meaning, and a compound considering his intention of the compositional meaning. So let it be considered.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage defines the compound (murakkab) term and addresses objections regarding its definition's exclusivity. The definition states: 'the first [type of expression] is that whose part indicates a part of its meaning, contrary to what follows.' The commentary explains that 'ma' is a relative pronoun, and the phrase 'whose part indicates' excludes terms that have no parts (like the letters ba and lam of the preposition) or have parts that do not indicate (like the name 'Zayd'). An objection is raised that this definition is not exclusive (ghayr mani') because it would include examples like 'Abdullah' (a compound proper name), 'rational animal' (a descriptive phrase), and 'Hujjat al-Islam' used as a proper name (where the coiner intended the meaning 'proof of Islam'). The author's answer is that the definition is intended to refer to cases where the part's indication is primary and intended (dalāla maqsūda bil-asāla). In 'Abdullah' and 'rational animal', the indication of the parts is not intended when used as a proper name or as a description respectively; in 'Hujjat al-Islam' as a proper name, the indication of the parts is intended but only secondarily, not primarily. Al-Mallawi offers a different answer: for 'Abdullah' and 'rational animal' when used as proper names, the parts do not indicate at all, so they are excluded by the condition 'its part indicates'. As for 'Hujjat al-Islam', he considers it a compound, not a simple term, so it should be included in the definition of compound and thus the objection that it is wrongly excluded is invalid. Some investigators reconcile by saying that such a term is simple from one perspective (considering the proper meaning) and compound from another (considering the compositional meaning). The passage thus defends the definition by clarifying that the indication of parts must be primary and intended.

Key Terms
مُرَكَّب
دَلَالَة مَقْصُودَة بِالأَصَالَة
عَلَمِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a valid compound term according to the definition in this passage?
A) 'Abdullah' used as a proper name.
B) 'Zayd' (a simple proper name).
C) 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' (the rational animal) in a philosophical context where both parts are intended.
D) 'Hujjat al-Islam' used as a proper name with secondary indication.

Key Takeaway
The definition of compound term requires that the part indicates a part of the meaning primarily and intentionally; proper names and secondary indications do not count.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Classify each of the following as mufrad or murakkab according to the definition: (a) 'al-Karim' as a name for a generous person; (b) 'al-karim al-rahim' (the Generous, the Merciful) used in supplication; (c) 'al-Hasan' as a proper name. Justify your answer with reference to primary intention.
?
Analyze the term 'al-Din al-Islami' (the Islamic religion). Is it a compound? If so, under what condition? If not, why not? Use the distinction between primary and secondary indication.

The Definition of Compound and Prima Facie Objections
Definition of compound with objections and responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish compound expressions from single words using the condition of primary indication, and identify proper names and descriptive compounds that might be mistaken for genuine compounds.

Core Idea
A compound is not just any multi-word phrase—its parts must intend their meanings primarily.

In Islamic logic, the division of utterances into mufrad (single) and murakkab (compound) is foundational for definitions. This lesson unpacks the definition of murakkab and resolves apparent objections, sharpening your ability to classify expressions correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definition
Locate the term being defined (murakkab) and the definition given: 'what whose part indicates a part of its meaning'. Note that this is the first type in the division of utterances.
The definition is the starting point for the entire discussion.
2
Understand what is excluded
The definition excludes words with no parts (like prepositions) and words with parts that do not indicate meaning (like the proper name 'Zayd'). Recognize that 'indicating' here means conveying a part of the overall meaning.
3
Recognize the objection
The objection claims the definition is not exclusive because it would include compound proper names ('Abdullah'), descriptive phrases ('al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq'), and titles used as names ('Ḥujjat al-Islam'), since each part does indicate a part of the meaning.
These examples seem to fit the definition, so why are they not compounds?
4
Examine the first answer: primary intention
Al-Bajuri answers that the indication must be intended primarily (bil-aṣāla). In 'Abdullah' as a name, the parts indicate only derivatively; the primary intention is the label, not the literal meanings. In 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' used as a definition, the indication is primary, so it remains a compound. For 'Ḥujjat al-Islam', the indication of 'proof' is intended but not primarily (bil-tabaʿ).
This refinement saves the definition.
5
Examine the second answer: proper names lose indication
Al-Mallawi argues that when expressions like 'Abdullah' become proper names, their parts cease to indicate any part of the meaning altogether. The indication we perceive is only before they became proper names. Hence they are excluded by the phrase 'whose part indicates'.
This is a different angle: the state of being a proper name (ʿalamiyya) nullifies the indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مباحث الألفاظ (٢٧) فَأَوَّلٌ : مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَ جُزءٍ مَعْنَاهُ بِعَكْسِ مَا تَلَا الباجوري قَوْلُهُ : (مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ ... إلخ) أي : الَّذِي أَوْ لَفْظُ دَلَّ ... إلخ، وَ مَا : مَوْصُولَةٌ، أَوْ مَوْصُوفَةٌ . وَخَرَجَ بِقَوْلِهِ : مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ مَا لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ بِأَنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ جُزءٌ أَصْلاً : بَاءِ الجَرِّ وَلَامِهِ، أَوْ لَهُ جُزءٌ لَكِنْ لَا يَدُلُّ كَـ : «زَيْدِ». وَاعْتُرِضَ عَلَى المُصَنِّفِ : بِأَنَّ هَذَا التَّعْرِيفَ غَيْرُ مَانِعِ ؛ لِشُمُولِهِ نَحْوَ : «عَبْدُ اللَّهِ» وَ: «الحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ وَ : «حُجَّةُ الإِسْلَامِ عَلَماً، مَعَ قَصْدِ الوَاضِعِ فِي الأَخِيرِ أَنَّ المُسَمَّى حُجَّةٌ فِي الدِّيْنِ. وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُرَادَ مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ دَلَالَةٌ مَقْصُودَةٌ بِالأَصَالَةِ، وَلَا كَذَلِكَ الدَّلَالَةُ فِيمَا ذُكِرَ؛ لِأَنَّهَا فِيمَا عَدَا الأَخِيرِ غَيْرُ مَقْصُودَةٍ، وَفِي الأَخِيرِ غَيْرُ مَقْصُودَةِ بِالأَصَالَةِ، بَلْ بِالتَّبع. وَأَجَابَ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ : بِأَنَّ مَا عَدَا الأَخِيرِ لَا يَدُلُّ جُزْؤُهُ حَالَ العَلَمِيَّةِ، فَهُوَ خَارِجُ بِقَوْلِهِ : «دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ، وَأَمَّا مَا يُتَوَهَّمُ مِنْ دَلَالَتِهِ فَإِنَّمَا هُوَ قَبْلَ العَلَمِيَّةِ (١) ، وَاخْتَارَ أَنَّ الأَخِيرَ مُرَكَّبٌ لَا مُفْرَدٌ (٢) ، فَلَا يَصِحُ إِخْرَاجُهُ لِوُجُوبِ إِدْخَالِهِ حِينَئِذٍ ، وَاخْتَارَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ أَنَّهُ مُفْرَدٌ بِاعْتِبَارِ قَصْدِ الوَاضِعِ المَعْنَى العَلَمِيِّ، وَمُرَكَّبٌ بِاعْتِبَارِ قَصْدِهِ المَعْنَى التَّرْكِيبِيِّ (٣)، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ .
Chapter on Investigations of Utterances. (27) The first [of the three types] is that whose part indicates a part of its meaning, contrary to what follows. al-Bajuri: His saying: 'whose part indicates' etc., i.e., the word which indicates... etc., and 'ma' is a relative pronoun or described noun. By his saying 'whose part indicates' is excluded that which is not like that, either by having no part at all — like the preposition 'ba' and its 'lam' — or having a part but it does not indicate, like 'Zayd'. And an objection was raised against the author: that this definition is not exclusive, because it includes such as 'Abdullah', 'the rational animal', and 'Hujjat al-Islam' as a proper name, with the intention of the one who coined it that the named is a proof in religion. And it was answered: that what is meant is that whose part indicates an indication intended primarily, and the indication in what was mentioned is not like that; because in those other than the last it is not intended, and in the last it is not intended primarily but rather derivatively. And al-Mallawi answered: that those other than the last do not have their parts indicating when they are proper names, so they are excluded by his saying 'whose part indicates'; as for what might be thought of their indication, it is only before they became proper names. And he chose that the last [i.e., Hujjat al-Islam] is a compound, not a single word, so it is not correct to exclude it because it must then be included. And some of the verifying scholars chose that it is a single word with respect to the coiner's intention of the proper meaning, and a compound with respect to his intention of the compositional meaning. So let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of expression (al-murakkab, compound) in the division of utterances into mufrad (single) and murakkab (compound). The definition states that a compound is that whose part indicates a part of its meaning, unlike a single word. The commentary by al-Bajuri explains that the phrase 'whose part indicates' excludes words that have no parts (like particles) and words that have parts but those parts do not indicate any part of the meaning (like the proper name 'Zayd'). An objection is then raised: the definition is not exclusive because it includes compound proper names like 'Abdullah' (servant of Allah), descriptive phrases like 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' (the rational animal), and titles used as proper names like 'Ḥujjat al-Islām' (Proof of Islam), since each part does indicate a part of the meaning. The first answer, attributed to al-Bajuri, clarifies that the intended indication must be primary and original (bil-aṣāla); in 'Abdullah' as a proper name, the indication of 'servant' and 'Allah' is not the primary intention—the word is simply a label. In 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq', though descriptive, the indication is not intended primarily when used as a definition. For 'Ḥujjat al-Islām' as a title, the indication of 'proof' is intended but derivatively (bi-l-tabaʿ), not primarily. The second answer, from al-Mallawi, argues that when expressions like 'Abdullah' become proper names (ʿalamiyya), their parts no longer indicate any part of the meaning at all; any perceived indication is only before they became proper names. He further holds that 'Ḥujjat al-Islām' is actually a compound, not a single word, so it should be included in the definition. Finally, some verifying scholars reconcile these views by saying that such a title is a single word from the perspective of its proper name designation, but a compound from the perspective of its compositional meaning. The passage ends by inviting reflection. This discussion is crucial in Islamic logic for distinguishing between single words and compounds, affecting how definitions (ḥudūd) are structured in disciplines like fiqh and uṣūl al-fiqh.

The Definition of Murakkab and the Near Part Distinction
Definition of murakkab with resolution of a definitional objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between near and distant parts in a compound expression and apply this distinction to evaluate the definition of mufrad.

Core Idea
The definition of murakkab hinges on the near part, not every component.

In this passage, the author defines murakkab (compound) as an expression whose part indicates a part of its meaning. He then addresses a potential objection against the definition of mufrad (simple): if a letter like 'zāy' in 'Zayd is standing' is a part that does not indicate a part of the meaning, wouldn't that make the phrase simple? The solution lies in distinguishing between near parts (direct components) and distant parts (components of components). This distinction is crucial for understanding how definitions work in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Find the definitions of murakkab and mufrad in the Arabic. Note that murakkab is 'that whose part indicates a part of its meaning' and mufrad is its opposite. Observe that they are opposites.
The matn defines murakkab as 'ما دل جزؤه على جزئ معناه بعكس ما تلا'.
2
Locate the objection
The objection is that the letter 'zāy' in 'Zayd is standing' is a part that does not indicate a part of the phrase's meaning, which would wrongly classify the phrase as mufrad.
The objector argues by counterexample against the definition of mufrad.
3
Understand the response
The response differentiates between 'near part' (al-juz' al-qarīb) – a direct component – and 'distant part' (al-juz' al-ba'īd) – a part of a component. The 'zāy' is a distant part because it is part of 'Zayd', which is a part of the phrase. The definitions refer only to near parts.
The rule 'part of a part is a part' holds but the definition specifies 'near part'.
4
Apply the distinction
Test other phrases: e.g., 'Zayd' is mufrad (its letters do not indicate parts of meaning), while 'Zayd is standing' is murakkab because 'Zayd' indicates the subject and 'standing' indicates the predicate.
Use this test for any simple or compound expression in Arabic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَأَوَّلٌ : مَا دَلَ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزُءِ مَعْنَاهُ بِعَكْسِ مَا تَلَا
قَوْلُهُ : (عَلَى جُزُءٍ مَعْنَاهُ) بِضَمِّ الزَّاي كَمَا قُرِئَ بِهِ فِي السَّبْعِ، وَهَذَا تَنْمِيمٌ لِلتَّعْرِيفِ
قَوْلُهُ : بِعَكْسِ مَا تَلَا يَعْنِي : أَنَّ المُرَكَّبَ مُلْتَبِسٌ بِعَكْسِ مَا تَلَاهُ؛ أَيْ: بِعَكْسِ المُفْرَدِ الَّذِي، أَوْ بِعَكْسِ مُفْرَدٍ تَلَاهُ ، وَالضَّمِيرُ المُسْتَتِرُ فِي تَلَا يَرْجِعُ لِـ «مَا » ، وَالضَّمِيرُ المُقَدَّرُ المَنْصُوبُ يَرْجِعُ لـ «المُرَكَبِ، هَذَا هُوَ الأَقْرَبُ المُوَافِقُ لِمَا هُوَ الوَاقِعُ مِنْ تَبَعِيَّةِ المُفْرَدِ لِلمُرَكَّب .
وَأَمَّا مَا يُصَرِّحُ بِهِ كَلَامُ المُصَنِّفِ فِي شَرْحِهِ مِنْ أَنَّ الضَّمِيرَ المُسْتَتِرَ يَرْجِعُ لـ «المُرَكَّب، وَالصَّمِيرَ المُقَدَّرَ المَنْصُوبَ يَرْجِعُ لـ «المُفْرَد» . فَهُوَ مَبْعُوثُ فِيهِ : بِأَنَّ الَّذِي تَلَا إِنَّمَا هُوَ المُفْرَدُ ، لَا المُرَكَّبُ ، وَبِأَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ لَوَجَبَ إِبْرَازُ الضَّمِيرِ ؛ لِجَرِّيَّانِ الصَّلَةِ أَوِ الصِّفَةِ عَلَى غَيْرِ مَا هِيَ لَهُ، مَعَ خَوْفِ اللَّبْسِ . وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُ أَرَادَ بِـ التّلْوِ»: الاتِّصَالَ مَجَازاً مُرْسَلاً ؛ لِعَلَاقَةِ : اللُّزُومِ، وَبِأَنَّ اللَّبْسَ هُنَا غَيْرُ مُضر ; لِصَّحَّةِ انّصْافِ كُلِّ مِنَ المُفْرَدِ وَالمُرَكَّبِ بِالتّلْوِ بِهَذَا المَعْنَى. لَكِنْ قَدْ يُعَكِّرُ عَلَى صَدْرِ الجَوَابِ : أَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ نَفْسَهُ فَسَّرَ تَلَا» بـ «تَبعَ»؛ إِلَّا أَنْ يُقَالَ : أَرَادَ بـ «تَبعَ » : اتَّصَلَ .
ثُمَّ قَالَ: وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ «العَكْسِ مَعْنَاهُ اللُّغَوِيُّ، وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ المُرَكَّبُ مُلْتَبِساً بِعَكْسِ مَا تَلَاهُ الَّذِي هُوَ المُفْرَدُ؛ لأَنَّهُمْ قَدْ عَرَّفُوا المُفْرَدَ بـ: «أَنَّهُ مَا لَا يَدُلُّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزءٍ مَعْنَاهُ، وَقَدْ عَرَّفَ هُوَ المُرَكَّبَ «أَنَّهُ مَا دَلَ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ ، وَلَا رَيْبَ أَنَّهُ عَكْسُ ذَلِكَ . لا يُقَالُ: يَرِدُ عَلَى تَعْرِيفِ المُفْرَدِ بِمَا ذُكِرَ : أَنَّ الزَّايَ مِنْ زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ، مَثَلاً لَا تَدُلُّ عَلَى جُزْء مَعْنَاهُ، فَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُفْرَداً . لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ : المُرَادُ بِـ «الجُزْءِ فِي قَوْلِنَا : مَا لَا يَدُلُّ ... إلخ » : الجُزْءُ القَرِيبُ، وَلَا كَذَلِكَ «الزَّاي) مِنْ زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ مَثَلاً ، فَإِنَّهَا جُزْءٌ بَعِيدٌ ; لأَنَّهَا إِنَّمَا كَانَتْ جُزْءاً بِوَاسِطَةِ أَنَّهَا جُزْءٌ مِنْ زَيْدِ» وَهُوَ جُزْءٌ مِنْ ذَلِكَ، وَالقَاعِدَةُ : «أَنَّ جُزْءَ جُزْءِ الشَّيْءِ جُزْءٌ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْء». هَذَا ; وَمَنْ نَكَّرَ الجُزْء بِأَنْ قَالَ: «لَا يَدُلُّ جُزْءٌ مِنْهُ ... إلخ» لَا يَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ ذَلِكَ; لِأَنَّ النَّكِرَةَ فِي سِيَاقِ النَّفْيِ تَعُمُّ ، فَيَخْرُجُ نَحْرُ المُرَكَّبِ المَذْكُورِ ; لِأَنَّ بَعْضَ أَجْزَائِهِ يَدُلُّ ، فَلْيُتَأمَّل.
The first is: that whose part indicates a part of its meaning, by the opposite of what follows. His statement: (upon a part of its meaning) with a dammah on the zāy, as it is recited in the seven readings, and this is a completion of the definition. His statement: by the opposite of what follows means: that the compound is characterized by the opposite of what preceded it; i.e., the opposite of the simple which, or the opposite of a simple that preceded it. The implicit pronoun in 'tala' refers to 'ma', and the estimated accusative pronoun refers to 'the compound'. This is the closest agreeing with what is actual, of the dependence of the simple on the compound. As for what the author's statement in his commentary explicitly says, that the implicit pronoun refers to 'the compound' and the estimated accusative pronoun refers to 'the simple', it is objectionable: because that which followed is only the simple, not the compound; and because if it were so, it would be necessary to manifest the pronoun; due to the running of the relative clause or adjective on other than its proper subject, with fear of confusion. And it was answered: that he intended by 'al-tallw' connection, metaphorically by metonymy, due to the relation of concomitance; and that confusion here is not harmful, because of the correctness of both the simple and the compound being described by 'following' in this sense. But it may be objected to the first part of the answer: that the author himself interpreted 'tala' as 'tabi'a'; unless it is said: he intended by 'tabi'a' connection. Then he said: And it is not hidden that what is meant by 'al-aks' is its linguistic meaning, and the compound is only characterized by the opposite of what preceded it, which is the simple; because they have defined the simple as: 'that whose part does not indicate a part of its meaning', and he defined the compound as 'that whose part indicates a part of its meaning', and there is no doubt that it is the opposite of that. It cannot be said: an objection arises against the definition of the simple by what was mentioned: that the zāy in 'Zayd is standing', for example, does not indicate a part of its meaning, so it would follow that it be simple. Because we say: what is meant by 'al-juz'' in our statement 'that whose part does not indicate...' is the near part, and it is not so with the zāy in 'Zayd is standing', for it is a distant part; because it is only a part by means of being a part of 'Zayd' which is a part of that, and the rule is: 'the part of the part of a thing is a part of that thing'. This; and whoever makes 'al-juz'' indefinite by saying 'no part of it indicates...' is not subject to that objection; because the indefinite in the context of negation is general, so the like of the mentioned compound is excluded; because some of its parts do indicate, so let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary. The matn defines murakkab (compound) as 'that whose part indicates a part of its meaning, by the opposite of what follows' (i.e., the opposite of the definition of mufrad). The commentator (al-Bajuri) first clarifies that the word 'جزء' in the matn is read with a dammah on the zāy, as in the seven Qira'at, and it is a completion (tankīm) of the definition, not a restriction. Then he explains 'بعكس ما تلا': the compound is characterized by the opposite of the preceding definition of mufrad. He discusses the pronouns in the phrase: the implied subject of 'تلا' refers to 'ما' (the simple), and the implied object refers to 'المركب' (the compound). This is the correct interpretation according to the author, because the simple is temporally prior and the compound depends on it. He then objects to another interpretation found in the author's own commentary, where the implied subject refers to the compound and the object to the simple. The objection is twofold: (1) it is the simple that 'follows' in the definition order, not the compound; (2) if the pronoun were reversed, the relative clause would run on a non-antecedent, causing confusion. The response is that 'تلا' is used metaphorically to mean 'connection' (ittiṣāl), not temporal sequence. The confusion is harmless because both the simple and compound can be described as 'connected' in this sense. However, a further objection arises: the author himself glossed 'تلا' as 'تبع' (followed), which implies sequence. The resolution is that 'تبع' is also meant metaphorically as connection. The passage then shifts to a broader explanation: the intended meaning of 'عكس' is linguistic (opposite), not technical. The compound is opposite to the simple definition. An objection is raised against the simple definition: the letter 'zāy' in 'Zayd is standing' is a part that does not indicate a part of the meaning of the phrase, so the phrase would be considered simple. The answer distinguishes between 'near part' (al-juz' al-qarīb) and 'distant part'. The 'zāy' is a distant part because it is a part of 'Zayd', which is itself a part of the phrase. The rule 'the part of a part is a part' applies, but the intended meaning in the definition is the near part. Alternatively, if one makes 'جزء' indefinite (tanwīn) in the definition, the objection does not arise because the indefinite in negation implies generality, excluding the compound where some parts do indicate.

Key Terms
مُرَكَّب
مُفْرَد
جُزْء
عَكْس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the intended meaning of 'juz’' (جزء) in the definition of mufrad?
A) Any part, including letters and words
B) The near part (al-juz' al-qarīb) that is a direct component
C) The distant part (al-juz' al-ba'īd) that is a part of a component
D) The part that is necessary for the meaning to be complete

Key Takeaway
The distinction between near and distant parts is essential for the definitions of mufrad and murakkab to hold.

Definition of Universal and Its Threefold Division
Commentator defines universal and particular, clarifies commonality, and presents threefold division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish ishtirak ma'nawi from ishtirak lafdhi and classify any universal into one of three divisions based on the existence of its individuals.

Core Idea
The universal indicates common meaning; it is divided into three by external existence.

This lesson unpacks the foundational definition of the universal and particular. You will not only learn the formal definition but also see how the ancient logicians classified universals based on whether their individuals exist in reality. The commentator Al-Bajuri helpfully clarifies that the intended commonality is in meaning, not in wording, a crucial distinction for later applications in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of universal and particular
Read the matn: 'What indicates commonality is the universal, like 'lion', and its opposite is the particular.' The universal is a concept that applies to multiple individuals, while the particular designates a single individual. Note the example: 'asad' (lion) applies to many lions.
Particulars are proper names or definite descriptions picking out one thing.
2
Distinguish ishtirak ma'nawi from ishtirak lafdhi
Al-Bajuri clarifies that the commonality meant in the definition is ishtirak ma'nawi: the word, its usage, and its meaning are one, but individuals sharing that meaning are many. Contrast with ishtirak lafdhi: same word, but different usage and meaning (homonyms).
For example, 'ayn' in Arabic has multiple unrelated meanings (eye, spring, spy) – that is lafdhi. But 'asad' for all lions is ma'nawi.
3
Learn the threefold division of the universal
The ancients divided universals into: (1) those with no existing individuals (e.g., phoenix), (2) those with only one existing individual (e.g., sun in geocentric model), and (3) those with multiple existing individuals (e.g., human).
This division is based on actual external existence, not mere conceptual existence.
4
Apply the division to Islamic examples
Take the universal 'malak' (angel) – it has many individuals in Islamic belief, so it is of the third type. For a universal like 'al-Bari' (the Creator) as a common noun? It is unique, so second type. Think of a universal that has no external individuals, like 'shay' (thing) is too broad; but a more specific example: 'jinn' has many individuals, so third type.
Ensure examples are from Islamic sciences and familiar to you.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الباجوري
وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ المُرَادَ : الاشْتِرَاكُ المَعْنَوِيُّ، وَضَابِطهُ : أَنْ يَتَّحِدَ اللَّفْظُ وَالوَضْعُ وَالمَعْنَى ، وَتَتَعَدَّدَ : الأَفْرَادُ المُشْتَرَكَةُ فِي ذَلِكَ المَعْنَى ، لَا اللَّفْظِيُّ، وَضَابِطُهُ : أَنْ يَتَّحِدَ اللَّفْظُ، وَيَتَعَدَّدَ الوَضْعُ وَالمَعْنَى .
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ أَقْسَامَ الكُلِّيَّ ثَلَاثَةٌ :
(١) - الأَوَّلُ : مَا لَمْ يُوْجَدْ مِنْهُ شَيْءٌ . -
(٢) - وَالثَّانِي: مَا وُجِدَ مِنْهُ فَرْدٌ وَاحِدٌ فَقَط . (۳) - وَالثَّالِثُ : مَا وُجِدَ مِنْهُ أَفْرَادٌ؛ كَذَا قَالَ الأَقْدَمُونَ.
(29) So what indicates commonality is the universal, like 'lion', and its opposite is the particular. Al-Bajuri: And it is not hidden that what is intended is commonality of meaning (ishtirak ma'nawi), whose definition is that the word, the usage, and the meaning are one, while the individuals participating in that meaning are multiple – not commonality of wording (ishtirak lafdhi), whose definition is that the word is one while the usage and meaning are multiple. And know that the divisions of the universal are three: (1) The first: that from which nothing exists. (2) The second: that from which only one individual exists. (3) The third: that from which multiple individuals exist. Thus said the ancients.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage opens the commentary on the matn line defining the universal and particular. The commentator first clarifies that the intended commonality is ishtirak ma'nawi: the word and its usage and meaning are the same, but the individuals sharing that meaning are many. This is contrasted with ishtirak lafdhi where the same word has different meanings and usages. For example, 'asad' as a universal noun indicates a category (lion) applicable to many individual lions. The particular, like 'Zayd', refers to a single individual and does not indicate commonality. The commentator then presents the division of the universal according to the earlier scholars (aqdamun) into three: (1) universals that have no existing individuals at all, like 'phoenix' in a world where it doesn't exist; (2) universals that have only one existing individual, like 'sun' in a geocentric model; and (3) universals that have multiple existing individuals, like 'human'. This threefold classification is based on actual existence of individuals in the external world.

الجُزْئِي
الاشتراك المعنوي

Key Takeaway
The universal is defined by indicating common meaning, and it is classified by the ancients into three types based on the external existence of its individuals: none, one, or many.

The Particular and the Priority of the Universal
Commentator defines the particular and explains why the universal is defined first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the particular (al-juz'i) and explain why the universal is prioritized in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The universal is the foundation of all definitions and proofs, hence defined first.

In this passage, the commentator defines the particular and then explains why the author placed the definition of the universal before that of the particular. This seemingly simple ordering reflects a deep logical principle: the universal is the material of definitions and proofs. Understanding this priority helps us appreciate the structure of Islamic logical texts and the role of universals in building knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of the particular
Find in the Arabic the definition of the particular (al-juz'i): it is what does not indicate commonality, like 'Zayd'. Note that the particular applies to a single individual.
The commentator explicitly gives the example 'Zayd'.
2
Distinguish accidental homonymy from intended commonality
The passage notes that accidental homonymy (e.g., two persons named Zayd) does not affect the definition, because the intended commonality is in meaning (ishtirak ma'nawi), not in word (ishtirak lafdhi).
This distinction is crucial for understanding the definition's precision.
3
Extract the reason for prioritizing the universal
The author states the universal is given priority (1) out of concern for it, because it is always the matter (mādda) of definitions, and mostly of demonstrations and conclusions. This reason is directly stated in the passage.
The commentator al-Anbabi expands on these terms in the next step.
4
Analyze the commentator's expansion on terminology
Al-Anbabi explains that 'definitions' (ḥudūd) here means all definitions, and 'demonstrations' (barāhīn) means all syllogisms (taghlīb). Alternatively, it may refer specifically to real definitions and real demonstrations due to their nobility. 'Conclusions' (maṭālib) are the results sought by proofs.
This expansion shows the commentator's effort to clarify the author's intended scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَعَكْسُهُ : «الجُزْئِيُّ» فَهُوَ مَا لَا يُفْهِمُ الاشْتِرَاكَ كَـ: «زَيْدِ»، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يُفْهِمُ الاشْتِرَاكَ. وَلَا عِبْرَةَ بِمَا يَعْرِضُ لَهُ مِنَ الاشْتِرَاكِ اللَّفْظِيّ; لِمَا تَقَدَّمَ مِنْ أَنَّ المُرَادَ هُنَا : الاشْتِرَاكُ المَعْنَوِيُّ. وَإِنَّمَا قَدَّمَ المُصَنِّفُ تَعْرِيفَ الكُلِّيِّ عَلَى تَعْرِيفِ الجُزْئِيِّ :

الأنبابي
قوله : (مَادَّةَ الحُدُودِ دَائِماً ، وَالبَرَاهِينِ أراد بـ «الحدود» : مطلق التعاريف، وبـ «البراهين»: مطلق الأقيسة، ففي كلامه تغليب [ب/ ٢٤] .

His saying: 'and its opposite is the particular' – so it is what does not indicate commonality, like 'Zayd', for it does not indicate commonality. And no attention is paid to what occurs to it of homonymy (ishtirak lafdhi), because it has preceded that what is intended here is commonality of meaning (ishtirak ma'nawi). And the author only presented the definition of the universal before the definition of the particular: (1) out of concern for it, because it is always the matter (material) of definitions, and mostly of demonstrations and conclusions. Al-Anbabi says: His saying 'the matter of definitions always and of demonstrations' – he intended by 'definitions' all definitions, and by 'demonstrations' all syllogisms, so in his speech there is a predominance. Or the intended meaning is real definitions and real demonstrations, so his specification of them is due to their nobility. And his saying 'and conclusions' (al-matālib) are the results, because they are sought by the proof. End (from al-Sabban).

Commentary
This passage completes the definitional section by explaining the particular (juz'i) and why the universal is treated first. The particular is defined as that which does not indicate commonality – it refers to a single individual, like 'Zayd'. The commentator stresses that accidental homonymy (like two persons named Zayd) does not affect this definition, because the intended commonality is ma'nawi, not lafdhi. The reason for presenting the universal before the particular is twofold: first, out of care for it (ihtimaman bihī), because the universal is the material (mādda) of definitions always, and of demonstrations and conclusions in most cases. The commentator al-Anbabi explains that 'definitions' here means all definitions, and 'demonstrations' means all syllogisms; there is a predominance (taghlīb) in the wording. Alternatively, it may refer specifically to real definitions and real demonstrations due to their nobility. The 'conclusions' (al-matālib) are the results sought by proofs. Thus, the universal is foundational for logical reasoning.
